<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Iron</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>

<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the metallic element. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Iron_(disambiguation)" title="Iron (disambiguation)">Iron (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 26</div><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption><span style="white-space:nowrap">Iron,  <sub><span style="font-size:smaller;">26</span></sub>Fe</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">

lustrous metallic with a grayish tinge</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Standard_atomic_weight" title="Standard atomic weight">Standard atomic weight</a><span style="font-weight:normal;"> (<i>A</i><sub>r, standard</sub>)</span></th><td>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_iron" title="Template:Infobox iron"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_iron" title="Template talk:Infobox iron"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_iron&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Iron</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Fe</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ferrum" title="wikt:ferrum">ferrum</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 26. It is a <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_transition_series" title="First transition series">first transition series</a>. It is by mass the most common element on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, forming much of Earth's <a href="/wiki/Outer_core" title="Outer core">outer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inner_core" title="Inner core">inner core</a>. It is the fourth most common <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth's crust">element in the Earth's crust</a>. Its abundance in <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">rocky planets</a> like Earth is due to its abundant production by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">fusion</a> in high-mass <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, where it is the last element to be produced with release of energy before the violent collapse of a <a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova" title="Type II supernova">supernova</a>, which scatters the iron into space.
</p><p>Like the other <a href="/wiki/Group_8_element" title="Group 8 element">group 8 elements</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium" title="Ruthenium">ruthenium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">osmium</a>, iron exists in a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a>, −2 to +7, although +2 and +3 are the most common. Elemental iron occurs in <a href="/wiki/Meteoroid" title="Meteoroid">meteoroids</a> and other low <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> environments, but is reactive to oxygen and <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>. Fresh iron surfaces appear lustrous silvery-gray, but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidize" title="Oxidize">oxidize</a> in normal air to give <a href="/wiki/Hydrate" title="Hydrate">hydrated</a> <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">iron oxides</a>, commonly known as <a href="/wiki/Rust" title="Rust">rust</a>. Unlike the metals that form <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivating</a> oxide layers, iron oxides occupy more volume than the metal and thus flake off, exposing fresh surfaces for corrosion.
</p><p>Iron metal has been used since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_age" title="Iron age">ancient times</a>, although <a href="/wiki/List_of_copper_alloys" title="List of copper alloys">copper alloys</a>, which have lower melting temperatures, were used even earlier in human history. Pure iron is relatively soft, but is unobtainable by <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a> because it is significantly hardened and strengthened by impurities, in particular <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, from the smelting process. A certain proportion of carbon (between 0.002% and 2.1%) produces <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>, which may be up to 1000 times harder than pure iron. Crude iron metal is produced in <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnaces</a>, where ore is reduced by <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a> to <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a>, which has a high carbon content. Further refinement with oxygen reduces the carbon content to the correct proportion to make steel. Steels and iron <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> formed with other metals (<a href="/wiki/Alloy_steel" title="Alloy steel">alloy steels</a>) are by far the most common industrial metals because they have a great range of desirable properties and iron-bearing rock is abundant.
</p><p>Iron chemical compounds have many uses. Iron oxide mixed with aluminium powder can be ignited to create a <a href="/wiki/Thermite" title="Thermite">thermite reaction</a>, used in welding and purifying ores. Iron forms binary compounds with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halogens" title="Halogens">halogens</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chalcogens" title="Chalcogens">chalcogens</a>. Among its organometallic compounds is <a href="/wiki/Ferrocene" title="Ferrocene">ferrocene</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Sandwich_compound" title="Sandwich compound">sandwich compound</a> discovered.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Iron#Biological_and_pathological_role" title="Iron">Iron plays an important role in biology</a>, forming complexes with molecular oxygen in <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a>; these two compounds are common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxygen_transport" title="Oxygen transport">oxygen transport</a> proteins in vertebrates. Iron is also the metal at the active site of many important <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">redox</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enzymes" title="Enzymes">enzymes</a> dealing with <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">cellular respiration</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation_and_reduction" title="Oxidation and reduction">oxidation and reduction</a> in plants and animals. In adult human males are some 3.8 grams of iron, and 2.3 grams in females, for whom iron is distributed in <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> and throughout the body. Iron is a critical element in the <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> of hundreds of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> involved in diverse body functions, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxygen_transport" title="Oxygen transport">oxygen transport</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> synthesis, and <a href="/wiki/Cell_growth" title="Cell growth">cell growth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>The mechanical properties of iron and its alloys can be evaluated using a variety of tests, including the <a href="/wiki/Brinell_scale" title="Brinell scale">Brinell test</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rockwell_scale" title="Rockwell scale">Rockwell test</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vickers_hardness_test" title="Vickers hardness test">Vickers hardness test</a>. The data on iron is so consistent that it is often used to calibrate measurements or to compare tests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corr_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-corr-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> However, the mechanical properties of iron are significantly affected by the sample's purity: pure, single crystals of iron are actually softer than aluminium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pure_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-pure-6">[6]</a></sup> and the purest industrially produced iron (99.99%) has a hardness of 20–30 Brinell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> An increase in the carbon content will cause a significant increase in the hardness and tensile strength of iron. Maximum hardness of <a href="/wiki/Rockwell_scale" title="Rockwell scale">65 R<sub>c</sub></a> is achieved with a 0.6% carbon content, although the alloy has low tensile strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Because of the softness of iron, it is much easier to work with than its heavier <a href="/wiki/Congener_(chemistry)" title="Congener (chemistry)">congeners</a> <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium" title="Ruthenium">ruthenium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">osmium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1074_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1074-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iron-alpha-pV.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="720" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Iron-alpha-pV.svg/240px-Iron-alpha-pV.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Iron-alpha-pV.svg/360px-Iron-alpha-pV.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Iron-alpha-pV.svg/480px-Iron-alpha-pV.svg.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iron-alpha-pV.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Molar volume vs. pressure for α iron at room temperature</div></div></div>
<p>Because of its significance for planetary cores, the physical properties of iron at high pressures and temperatures have also been studied extensively. The form of iron that is stable under standard conditions can be subjected to pressures up to ca. 15 GPa before transforming into a high-pressure form, as described in the next section.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phase_diagram_and_allotropes">Phase diagram and allotropes</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_iron" title="Allotropes of iron">Allotropes of iron</a></div>
<p>Iron represents an example of <a href="/wiki/Allotropy" title="Allotropy">allotropy</a> in a metal. At least four allotropic forms of iron are known as α, γ, δ, and ε; at very high pressures and temperatures, some controversial experimental evidence exists for a stable β phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beta-iron_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-beta-iron-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pure_iron_phase_diagram_(EN).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="714" data-file-width="810" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Pure_iron_phase_diagram_%28EN%29.png/240px-Pure_iron_phase_diagram_%28EN%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Pure_iron_phase_diagram_%28EN%29.png/360px-Pure_iron_phase_diagram_%28EN%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Pure_iron_phase_diagram_%28EN%29.png/480px-Pure_iron_phase_diagram_%28EN%29.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pure_iron_phase_diagram_(EN).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Low-pressure <a href="/wiki/Phase_diagram" title="Phase diagram">phase diagram</a> of pure iron</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Magnetization_curves.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="780" data-file-width="680" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Magnetization_curves.svg/220px-Magnetization_curves.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Magnetization_curves.svg/330px-Magnetization_curves.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Magnetization_curves.svg/440px-Magnetization_curves.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Magnetization_curves.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Magnetization curves of 9 ferromagnetic materials, showing saturation. 1.<span class="nowrap"> </span>Sheet steel, 2.<span class="nowrap"> </span>Silicon steel, 3.<span class="nowrap"> </span>Cast steel, 4.<span class="nowrap"> </span>Tungsten steel, 5.<span class="nowrap"> </span>Magnet steel, 6.<span class="nowrap"> </span>Cast iron, 7.<span class="nowrap"> </span>Nickel, 8.<span class="nowrap"> </span>Cobalt, 9.<span class="nowrap"> </span>Magnetite<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>As molten iron cools past its freezing point of 1538 °C, it crystallizes into its δ allotrope, which has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body-centered_cubic" title="Body-centered cubic">body-centered cubic</a> (bcc) <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">crystal structure</a>. As it cools further to 1394 °C, it changes to its γ-iron allotrope, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Face-centered_cubic" title="Face-centered cubic">face-centered cubic</a> (fcc) crystal structure, or <a href="/wiki/Austenite" title="Austenite">austenite</a>. At 912 °C and below, the crystal structure again becomes the bcc α-iron allotrope. Finally, at 770 °C (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curie_point" title="Curie point">Curie point</a>, T<sub>c</sub>) iron's magnetic ordering changes from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paramagnetic" title="Paramagnetic">paramagnetic</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferromagnetic" title="Ferromagnetic">ferromagnetic</a>. As is passes through the Curie temperature, iron does not change its structure, but "magnetic domains" appear, where each domain contains iron atoms with a particular electronic spin. In unmagnetized iron, all the electronic spins of the atoms within one domain have the same axis orientation; however, the electrons of neighboring domains have other orientations with the result of mutual cancellation and no magnetic field. In magnetized iron, the electronic spins of the domains are aligned and the magnetic effects are reinforced. Although each domain contains billions of atoms, they are very small, about 10 micrometres across.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metallo_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metallo-14">[14]</a></sup> This happens because the two unpaired electrons on each iron atom are in the d<sub><i>z</i><sup>2</sup></sub> and d<sub><i>x</i><sup>2</sup> − <i>y</i><sup>2</sup></sub> orbitals, which do not point directly at the nearest neighbors in the body-centered cubic lattice and therefore do not participate in metallic bonding; thus, they can interact magnetically with each other so that their spins align.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1075_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1075-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>At pressures above approximately 10 GPa and temperatures of a few hundred kelvin or less, α-iron changes into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexagonal_close-packed" title="Hexagonal close-packed">hexagonal close-packed</a> (hcp) structure, which is also known as <a href="/wiki/Hexaferrum" title="Hexaferrum">ε-iron</a>; the higher-temperature γ-phase also changes into ε-iron, but does so at higher pressure. The β-phase, if it exists, would appear at pressures of at least 50 GPa and temperatures of at least 1500 K and have an orthorhombic or a double hcp structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beta-iron_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-beta-iron-12">[12]</a></sup> These high-pressure phases of iron are important as endmember models for the solid parts of planetary cores. The <a href="/wiki/Inner_core" title="Inner core">inner core</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> is generally presumed to be an iron-<a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloy</a> with ε (or β) structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Somewhat confusingly, the term "β-iron" is sometimes also used to refer to α-iron above its Curie point, when it changes from being ferromagnetic to paramagnetic, even though its crystal structure has not changed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1075_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1075-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The melting point of iron is experimentally well defined for pressures less than 50 GPa. For greater pressures, studies put the γ-ε-liquid <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> at pressures that differ by tens of gigapascals and 1000 K in the melting point. Generally speaking, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_dynamics" title="Molecular dynamics">molecular dynamics</a> computer simulations of iron melting and shock wave experiments suggest higher melting points and a much steeper slope of the melting curve than static experiments carried out in <a href="/wiki/Diamond_anvil_cell" title="Diamond anvil cell">diamond anvil cells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-melting_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-melting-17">[17]</a></sup> The melting and boiling points of iron, along with its <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy_of_atomization" title="Enthalpy of atomization">enthalpy of atomization</a>, are lower than those of the earlier 3d elements from <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">scandium</a> to <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a>, showing the lessened contribution of the 3d electrons to metallic bonding as they are attracted more and more into the inert core by the nucleus;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1116_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1116-18">[18]</a></sup> however, they are higher than the values for the previous element <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> because that element has a half-filled 3d subshell and consequently its d-electrons are not easily delocalized. This same trend appears for ruthenium but not osmium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1074_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1074-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_iron" title="Isotopes of iron">Isotopes of iron</a></div>
<p>Naturally occurring iron consists of four stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>: 5.845% of <sup>54</sup>Fe, 91.754% of <a href="/wiki/Iron-56" title="Iron-56"><sup>56</sup>Fe</a>, 2.119% of <sup>57</sup>Fe and 0.282% of <sup>58</sup>Fe. Of these stable isotopes, only <sup>57</sup>Fe has a nuclear <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a> (−​<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span>). The <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclide</a> <sup>54</sup>Fe theoretically can undergo <a href="/wiki/Double_beta_decay" title="Double beta decay">double electron capture</a> to <sup>54</sup>Cr, but the process has never been observed and only a lower limit on the half-life of 3.1×10<sup>22</sup> years has been established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p><sup>60</sup>Fe is an <a href="/wiki/Extinct_radionuclide" title="Extinct radionuclide">extinct radionuclide</a> of long <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> (2.6 million years).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RugelFaestermann2009_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-RugelFaestermann2009-20">[20]</a></sup> It is not found on Earth, but its ultimate decay product is its granddaughter, the stable nuclide <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nickel-60" title="Nickel-60"><sup>60</sup>Ni</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-19">[19]</a></sup> Much of the past work on isotopic composition of iron has focused on the <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a> of <sup>60</sup>Fe through studies of <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorite</a>s and ore formation. In the last decade, advances in <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometry</a> have allowed the detection and quantification of minute, naturally occurring variations in the ratios of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stable_isotope" title="Stable isotope">stable isotopes</a> of iron. Much of this work is driven by the <a href="/wiki/Earth_science" title="Earth science">Earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Planetary_science" title="Planetary science">planetary science</a> communities, although applications to biological and industrial systems are emerging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>In phases of the meteorites <i>Semarkona</i> and <i>Chervony Kut,</i> a correlation between the concentration of <sup>60</sup>Ni, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daughter_product" title="Daughter product">granddaughter</a> of <sup>60</sup>Fe, and the abundance of the stable iron isotopes provided evidence for the existence of <sup>60</sup>Fe at the time of <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">formation of the Solar System</a>. Possibly the energy released by the decay of <sup>60</sup>Fe, along with that released by <a href="/wiki/Aluminium-26" title="Aluminium-26"><sup>26</sup>Al</a>, contributed to the remelting and <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">differentiation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> after their formation 4.6 billion years ago. The abundance of <sup>60</sup>Ni present in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/extraterrestrial" title="wikt:extraterrestrial">extraterrestrial</a> material may bring further insight into the origin and early history of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most abundant iron isotope <sup>56</sup>Fe is of particular interest to nuclear scientists because it represents the most common endpoint of <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Since <sup>56</sup>Ni (14 <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a>) is easily produced from lighter nuclei in the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_process" title="Alpha process">alpha process</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reactions</a> in supernovae (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicon_burning_process" title="Silicon burning process">silicon burning process</a>), it is the endpoint of fusion chains inside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_III_stars" title="Population III stars">extremely massive stars</a>, since addition of another alpha particle, resulting in <sup>60</sup>Zn, requires a great deal more energy. This <sup>56</sup>Ni, which has a half-life of about 6 days, is created in quantity in these stars, but soon decays by two successive positron emissions within supernova decay products in the <a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">supernova remnant</a> gas cloud, first to radioactive <sup>56</sup>Co, and then to stable <sup>56</sup>Fe. As such, iron is the most abundant element in the core of <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giants</a>, and is the most abundant metal in <a href="/wiki/Iron_meteorite" title="Iron meteorite">iron meteorites</a> and in the dense metal <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">cores of planets</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood12_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood12-24">[24]</a></sup> It is also very common in the universe, relative to other stable <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metals</a> of approximately the same <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">atomic weight</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood12_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood12-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WoosleyJanka_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-WoosleyJanka-25">[25]</a></sup> Iron is the sixth most <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">abundant element</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a>, and the most common <a href="/wiki/Refractory" title="Refractory">refractory</a> element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apjl717_2_L92_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-apjl717_2_L92-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although a further tiny energy gain could be extracted by synthesizing <a href="/wiki/Nickel-62" title="Nickel-62"><sup>62</sup>Ni</a>, which has a marginally higher binding energy than <sup>56</sup>Fe, conditions in stars are unsuitable for this process. Element production in supernovas and distribution on Earth greatly favor iron over nickel, and in any case, <sup>56</sup>Fe still has a lower mass per nucleon than <sup>62</sup>Ni due to its higher fraction of lighter protons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Hence, elements heavier than iron require a <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> for their formation, involving <a href="/wiki/R-process" title="R-process">rapid neutron capture</a> by starting <sup>56</sup>Fe nuclei.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood12_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood12-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>
In the <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_far_future" title="Timeline of the far future">far future</a> of the universe, assuming that <a href="/wiki/Proton_decay" title="Proton decay">proton decay</a> does not occur, cold <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">fusion</a> occurring via <a href="/wiki/Quantum_tunnelling" title="Quantum tunnelling">quantum tunnelling</a> would cause the light nuclei in ordinary matter to fuse into <sup>56</sup>Fe nuclei. Fission and <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha-particle emission</a> would then make heavy nuclei decay into iron, converting all stellar-mass objects to cold spheres of pure iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twoe_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-twoe-28">[28]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Widmanstatten_hand.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Widmanstatten_hand.jpg/220px-Widmanstatten_hand.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Widmanstatten_hand.jpg/330px-Widmanstatten_hand.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Widmanstatten_hand.jpg/440px-Widmanstatten_hand.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Widmanstatten_hand.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Iron_meteorite" title="Iron meteorite">Iron meteorites</a>, similar in composition to the Earth's inner- and outer core</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Iron_minerals" title="Category:Iron minerals">Category:Iron minerals</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roussillon_sentier_des_ocres2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1535" data-file-width="2293" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Roussillon_sentier_des_ocres2.JPG/220px-Roussillon_sentier_des_ocres2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Roussillon_sentier_des_ocres2.JPG/330px-Roussillon_sentier_des_ocres2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Roussillon_sentier_des_ocres2.JPG/440px-Roussillon_sentier_des_ocres2.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roussillon_sentier_des_ocres2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ochre" title="Ochre">Ochre</a> path in the <a href="/wiki/Roussillon,_Vaucluse" title="Roussillon, Vaucluse">Roussillon</a></div></div></div>
<p>Metallic or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_iron" title="Native iron">native iron</a> is rarely found on the surface of the Earth because it tends to oxidize, but its oxides are pervasive and represent the primary ores. While it makes up about 5% of the <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">Earth's crust</a>, both the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Inner_core" title="Inner core">inner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Outer_core" title="Outer core">outer core</a> are believed to consist largely of an iron-<a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> alloy constituting 35% of the mass of the Earth as a whole. Iron is consequently the most abundant element on Earth, but only the fourth most abundant element in the Earth's crust, after <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Most of the iron in the crust is found combined with oxygen as <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">iron oxide</a> minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Hematite" title="Hematite">hematite</a> (Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>), <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a> (Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>), and <a href="/wiki/Siderite" title="Siderite">siderite</a> (FeCO<sub>3</sub>). Many <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous rocks</a> also contain the sulfide minerals <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhotite" title="Pyrrhotite">pyrrhotite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pentlandite" title="Pentlandite">pentlandite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mindat_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-mindat-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klein_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klein-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ferropericlase" title="Ferropericlase">Ferropericlase</a> (Mg,Fe)O, a solid solution of <a href="/wiki/Periclase" title="Periclase">periclase</a> (MgO) and <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCstite" title="Wüstite">wüstite</a> (FeO), makes up about 20% of the volume of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_mantle" title="Lower mantle">lower mantle</a> of the Earth, which makes it the second most abundant mineral phase in that region after silicate <a href="/wiki/Perovskite" title="Perovskite">perovskite</a> (Mg,Fe)SiO<sub>3</sub>; it also is the major host for iron in the lower mantle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>   At the bottom of the <a href="/wiki/Transition_zone_(Earth)" title="Transition zone (Earth)">transition zone</a> of the mantle, the reaction γ-(Mg,Fe)<sub>2</sub>[SiO<sub>4</sub>] ↔ (Mg,Fe)[SiO<sub>3</sub>] + (Mg,Fe)O transforms <a href="/wiki/Ringwoodite" title="Ringwoodite">γ-olivine</a> into a mixture of perovskite and ferropericlase and vice versa. In the literature, this mineral phase of the lower mantle is also often called magnesiowüstite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferro_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ferro-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Silicate_perovskite" title="Silicate perovskite">Silicate perovskite</a> may form up to 93% of the lower mantle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murakami_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murakami-34">[34]</a></sup> and the magnesium iron form, (Mg,Fe)SiO<sub>3</sub>, is considered to be the most abundant <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">mineral</a> in the Earth, making up 38% of its volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sharp_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sharp-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Large deposits of iron are found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banded_iron_formations" title="Banded iron formations">banded iron formations</a>. These geological formations are a type of rock consisting of repeated thin layers of iron oxides alternating with bands of iron-poor <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">shale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chert" title="Chert">chert</a>. The banded iron formations were laid down in the time between <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=3,700">3,700</a> million years ago</span> and <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=1,800">1,800</a> million years ago</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>The mentioned iron compounds have been used as <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a>s (compare <a href="/wiki/Ochre" title="Ochre">ochre</a>) since historical time and contribute as well to the color of various geological formations, e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Buntsandstein" title="Buntsandstein">Buntsandstein</a> (British <a href="/wiki/Bunter_(geology)" title="Bunter (geology)">Bunter</a>, colored sandstein).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>  In the case of the Eisensandstein (a jurassic 'iron sandstone', e.g. from <a href="/wiki/Donzdorf" title="Donzdorf">Donzdorf</a>) in Germany<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bath_stone" title="Bath stone">Bath stone</a> in the UK, iron pigments contribute to the yellowish color of large amounts of historical buildings and sculptures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The proverbial <a href="/wiki/Mars_surface_color" title="Mars surface color">red color of the surface of Mars</a> is derived from an iron oxide-rich <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">regolith</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Significant amounts of iron occur in the iron sulfide mineral <a href="/wiki/Pyrite" title="Pyrite">pyrite</a> (FeS<sub>2</sub>), but it is difficult to extract iron from it and it is therefore not used. In fact, iron is so common that production generally focuses only on ores with very high quantities of it. During <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a>, iron tends to leach from sulfide deposits as the sulfate and from silicate deposits as the bicarbonate. Both of these are oxidized in aqueous solution and precipitate in even mildly elevated pH as <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide" title="Iron(III) oxide">iron(III) oxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1071_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1071-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>About 1 in 20 <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a> consist of the unique iron-nickel minerals <a href="/wiki/Taenite" title="Taenite">taenite</a> (35–80% iron) and <a href="/wiki/Kamacite" title="Kamacite">kamacite</a> (90–95% iron). Although rare, <a href="/wiki/Iron_meteorite" title="Iron meteorite">iron meteorites</a> are the main form of natural metallic iron on the Earth's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Resource_Panel" title="International Resource Panel">International Resource Panel</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Metal_Stocks_in_Society_report" title="Metal Stocks in Society report">Metal Stocks in Society report</a>, the global stock of iron in use in society is 2200 kg per capita. More-developed countries differ in this respect from less-developed countries (7000–14000 vs 2000 kg per capita).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry_and_compounds">Chemistry and compounds</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Iron_compounds" title="Category:Iron compounds">Category:Iron compounds</a>.</div>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; clear:right; margin-left:1em; margin-top:0;">

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Iron shows the characteristic chemical properties of the <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metals</a>, namely the ability to form variable oxidation states differing by steps of one and a very large coordination and organometallic chemistry: indeed, it was the discovery of an iron compound, <a href="/wiki/Ferrocene" title="Ferrocene">ferrocene</a>, that revolutionalized the latter field in the 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood905_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood905-45">[45]</a></sup> Iron is sometimes considered as a prototype for the entire block of transition metals, due to its abundance and the immense role it has played in the technological progress of humanity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1070_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1070-46">[46]</a></sup> Its 26 electrons are arranged in the <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">configuration</a> [Ar]3d<sup>6</sup>4s<sup>2</sup>, of which the 3d and 4s electrons are relatively close in energy, and thus it can lose a variable number of electrons and there is no clear point where further ionization becomes unprofitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1074_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1074-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Iron forms compounds mainly in the +2 and +3 <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a>. Traditionally, iron(II) compounds are called <a href="/wiki/Ferrous" title="Ferrous">ferrous</a>, and iron(III) compounds <a href="/wiki/Ferric" title="Ferric">ferric</a>. Iron also occurs in <a href="/wiki/High-valent_iron" title="High-valent iron">higher oxidation states</a>, e.g. the purple <a href="/wiki/Potassium_ferrate" title="Potassium ferrate">potassium ferrate</a> (K<sub>2</sub>FeO<sub>4</sub>), which contains iron in its +6 oxidation state. Although iron(VIII) oxide (FeO<sub>4</sub>) has been claimed, the report could not be reproduced and such a species (at least with iron in its +8 oxidation state) has been found to be improbable computationally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>  However, one form of anionic [FeO<sub>4</sub>]<sup>–</sup> with iron in its +7 oxidation state, along with an iron(V)-peroxo isomer, has been detected by infrared spectroscopy at 4 K after cocondensation of laser-ablated Fe atoms with a mixture of O<sub>2</sub>/Ar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Iron(IV) is a common intermediate in many biochemical oxidation reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-50">[50]</a></sup> Numerous <a href="/wiki/Organoiron_chemistry" title="Organoiron chemistry">organoiron</a> compounds contain formal oxidation states of +1, 0, −1, or even −2. The oxidation states and other bonding properties are often assessed using the technique of <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ssbauer_spectroscopy" title="Mössbauer spectroscopy">Mössbauer spectroscopy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
Many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixed_valence_compound" title="Mixed valence compound">mixed valence compounds</a> contain both iron(II) and iron(III) centers, such as <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prussian_blue" title="Prussian blue">Prussian blue</a> (Fe<sub>4</sub>(Fe[CN]<sub>6</sub>)<sub>3</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-50">[50]</a></sup> The latter is used as the traditional "blue" in <a href="/wiki/Blueprint" title="Blueprint">blueprints</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Iron is the first of the transition metals that cannot reach its group oxidation state of +8, although its heavier congeners ruthenium and osmium can, with ruthenium having more difficulty than osmium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1075_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1075-15">[15]</a></sup> Ruthenium exhibits an aqueous cationic chemistry in its low oxidation states similar to that of iron, but osmium does not, favoring high oxidation states in which it forms anionic complexes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1075_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1075-15">[15]</a></sup> In the second half of the 3d transition series, vertical similarities down the groups compete with the horizontal similarities of iron with its neighbors <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> in the periodic table, which are also ferromagnetic at <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a> and share similar chemistry. As such, iron, cobalt, and nickel are sometimes grouped together as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_triad" title="Iron triad">iron triad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1070_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1070-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iron(III)_chloride_hexahydrate.jpg"><img alt="Some canary-yellow powder sits, mostly in lumps, on a laboratory watch glass." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="434" data-file-width="490" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Iron%28III%29_chloride_hexahydrate.jpg/220px-Iron%28III%29_chloride_hexahydrate.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Iron%28III%29_chloride_hexahydrate.jpg/330px-Iron%28III%29_chloride_hexahydrate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Iron%28III%29_chloride_hexahydrate.jpg/440px-Iron%28III%29_chloride_hexahydrate.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iron(III)_chloride_hexahydrate.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hydrated <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_chloride" title="Iron(III) chloride">iron(III) chloride</a>, also known as ferric chloride</div></div></div>
<p>The iron compounds produced on the largest scale in industry are <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_sulfate" title="Iron(II) sulfate">iron(II) sulfate</a> (FeSO<sub>4</sub>·7<a href="/wiki/Water_of_crystallization" title="Water of crystallization">H<sub>2</sub>O</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_chloride" title="Iron(III) chloride">iron(III) chloride</a> (FeCl<sub>3</sub>). The former is one of the most readily available sources of iron(II), but is less stable to aerial oxidation than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohr%27s_salt" title="Mohr's salt">Mohr's salt</a> ((NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>Fe(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O). Iron(II) compounds tend to be oxidized to iron(III) compounds in the air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike many other metals, iron does not form amalgams with <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>. As a result, mercury is traded in standardized 76 pound flasks (34 kg) made of iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Iron is by far the most reactive element in its group; it is pyrophoric when finely divided and dissolves easily in dilute acids, giving Fe<sup>2+</sup>. However, it does not react with concentrated <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> and other oxidizing acids due to the formation of an impervious oxide layer, which can nevertheless react with <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1075_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1075-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Binary_compounds">Binary compounds</span></h3>
<p>Iron reacts with oxygen in the air to form various <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">oxide and hydroxide compounds</a>; the most common are <a href="/wiki/Iron(II,III)_oxide" title="Iron(II,III) oxide">iron(II,III) oxide</a> (Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>), and <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide" title="Iron(III) oxide">iron(III) oxide</a> (Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>). <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_oxide" title="Iron(II) oxide">Iron(II) oxide</a> also exists, though it is unstable at room temperature. Despite their names, they are actually all <a href="/wiki/Non-stoichiometric_compound" title="Non-stoichiometric compound">non-stoichiometric compounds</a> whose compositions may vary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1079_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1079-54">[54]</a></sup> These oxides are the principal ores for the production of iron (see <a href="/wiki/Bloomery" title="Bloomery">bloomery</a> and blast furnace). They are also used in the production of <a href="/wiki/Ferrite_(magnet)" title="Ferrite (magnet)">ferrites</a>, useful <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_storage" title="Magnetic storage">magnetic storage</a> media in computers, and pigments. The best known sulfide is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_pyrite" title="Iron pyrite">iron pyrite</a> (FeS<sub>2</sub>), also known as fool's gold owing to its golden luster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-50">[50]</a></sup> It is not an iron(IV) compound, but is actually an iron(II) <a href="/wiki/Polysulfide" title="Polysulfide">polysulfide</a> containing Fe<sup>2+</sup> and <span class="chemf nowrap">S<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> ions in a distorted <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1079_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1079-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pourbaix_Diagram_of_Iron.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="605" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Pourbaix_Diagram_of_Iron.svg/220px-Pourbaix_Diagram_of_Iron.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Pourbaix_Diagram_of_Iron.svg/330px-Pourbaix_Diagram_of_Iron.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Pourbaix_Diagram_of_Iron.svg/440px-Pourbaix_Diagram_of_Iron.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pourbaix_Diagram_of_Iron.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pourbaix_diagram" title="Pourbaix diagram">Pourbaix diagram</a> of iron</div></div></div>
<p>The binary ferrous and ferric halides are well-known, with the exception of ferric iodide. The ferrous halides typically arise from treating iron metal with the corresponding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrohalic_acid" title="Hydrohalic acid">hydrohalic acid</a> to give the corresponding hydrated salts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_50-4"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>Fe + 2 HX → FeX<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub> (X = F, Cl, Br, I)</dd></dl>
<p>Iron reacts with fluorine, chlorine, and bromine to give the corresponding ferric halides, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferric_chloride" title="Ferric chloride">ferric chloride</a> being the most common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1082_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1082-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 Fe + 3 X<sub>2</sub> → 2 FeX<sub>3</sub> (X = F, Cl, Br)</dd></dl>
<p>Ferric iodide is an exception, being thermodynamically unstable due to the oxidizing power of Fe<sup>3+</sup> and the high reducing power of I<sup>−</sup>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1082_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1082-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 I<sup>−</sup> + 2 Fe<sup>3+</sup> → I<sub>2</sub> + 2 Fe<sup>2+</sup> (E<sup>0</sup> = +0.23 V)</dd></dl>
<p>Nevertheless, milligram amounts of ferric iodide, a black solid, may still be prepared through the reaction of <a href="/wiki/Iron_pentacarbonyl" title="Iron pentacarbonyl">iron pentacarbonyl</a> with <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Hexane" title="Hexane">hexane</a> and light at the temperature of −20 °C, making sure that the system is well sealed off from air and water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1082_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1082-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solution_chemistry">Solution chemistry</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ferrate_and_permanganate_solution.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="657" data-file-width="500" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Ferrate_and_permanganate_solution.jpg/100px-Ferrate_and_permanganate_solution.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Ferrate_and_permanganate_solution.jpg/150px-Ferrate_and_permanganate_solution.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Ferrate_and_permanganate_solution.jpg/200px-Ferrate_and_permanganate_solution.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ferrate_and_permanganate_solution.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of colors of solutions of ferrate (left) and <a href="/wiki/Permanganate" title="Permanganate">permanganate</a> (right)</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_reduction_potential" title="Standard reduction potential">standard reduction potentials</a> in acidic aqueous solution for some common iron ions are given below:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1075_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1075-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<table>
<tbody><tr>
<td>Fe<sup>2+</sup> + 2 e<sup>−</sup></td>
<td>⇌ Fe</td>
<td>E<sup>0</sup> = −0.447 V
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Fe<sup>3+</sup> + 3 e<sup>−</sup></td>
<td>⇌ Fe</td>
<td>E<sup>0</sup> = −0.037 V
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span class="chemf nowrap">FeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 8 H<sup>+</sup> + 3 e<sup>−</sup></td>
<td>⇌ Fe<sup>3+</sup> + 4 H<sub>2</sub>O</td>
<td>E<sup>0</sup> = +2.20 V
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The red-purple tetrahedral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrate" title="Ferrate">ferrate</a>(VI) anion is such a strong oxidizing agent that it oxidizes nitrogen and ammonia at room temperature, and even water itself in acidic or neutral solutions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1082_55-3"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1082-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>4 <span class="chemf nowrap">FeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 10 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> → 4 <span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + 20 <span class="chemf nowrap">OH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + 3 O<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>The Fe<sup>3+</sup> ion has a large simple cationic chemistry, although the pale-violet hexaquo ion [Fe(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3+</sup> is very readily hydrolyzed when pH increases above 0 as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1088_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1088-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<table>
<tbody><tr>
<td>[Fe(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3+</sup></td>
<td>⇌ [Fe(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>5</sub>(OH)]<sup>2+</sup> + H<sup>+</sup></td>
<td><i><a href="/wiki/Equilibrium_constant" title="Equilibrium constant">K</a></i> = 10<sup>−3.05</sup> mol dm<sup>−3</sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>[Fe(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>5</sub>(OH)]<sup>2+</sup></td>
<td>⇌ [Fe(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>4</sub>(OH)<sub>2</sub>]<sup>+</sup> + H<sup>+</sup></td>
<td><i>K</i> = 10<sup>−3.26</sup> mol dm<sup>−3</sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>2 [Fe(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3+</sup></td>
<td>⇌ [Fe(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>(OH)]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span> + 2 H<sup>+</sup> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O</td>
<td><i>K</i> = 10<sup>−2.91</sup> mol dm<sup>−3</sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><img alt="" data-file-height="1666" data-file-width="2221" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Iron%28III%29-oxide-sample.jpg/200px-Iron%28III%29-oxide-sample.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Iron%28III%29-oxide-sample.jpg/300px-Iron%28III%29-oxide-sample.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Iron%28III%29-oxide-sample.jpg/400px-Iron%28III%29-oxide-sample.jpg 2x" width="200"/></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Dark red <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide" title="Iron(III) oxide">iron(III) oxide</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><img alt="" data-file-height="1504" data-file-width="2005" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Iron%28II%29-sulfate-heptahydrate-sample.jpg/200px-Iron%28II%29-sulfate-heptahydrate-sample.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Iron%28II%29-sulfate-heptahydrate-sample.jpg/300px-Iron%28II%29-sulfate-heptahydrate-sample.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Iron%28II%29-sulfate-heptahydrate-sample.jpg/400px-Iron%28II%29-sulfate-heptahydrate-sample.jpg 2x" width="200"/></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Blue-green <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_sulfate" title="Iron(II) sulfate">iron(II) sulfate</a> heptahydrate</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<p>As pH rises above 0 the above yellow hydrolyzed species form and as it rises above 2–3, reddish-brown hydrous <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide" title="Iron(III) oxide">iron(III) oxide</a> precipitates out of solution. Although Fe<sup>3+</sup> has an d<sup>5</sup> configuration, its absorption spectrum is not like that of Mn<sup>2+</sup> with its weak, spin-forbidden d–d bands, because Fe<sup>3+</sup> has higher positive charge and is more polarizing, lowering the energy of its ligand-to-metal <a href="/wiki/Charge-transfer_complex" title="Charge-transfer complex">charge transfer</a> absorptions. Thus, all the above complexes are rather strongly colored, with the single exception of the hexaquo ion – and even that has a spectrum dominated by charge transfer in the near ultraviolet region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1088_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1088-56">[56]</a></sup> On the other hand, the pale green iron(II) hexaquo ion [Fe(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>2+</sup> does not undergo appreciable hydrolysis. Carbon dioxide is not evolved when <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> anions are added, which instead results in white <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_carbonate" title="Iron(II) carbonate">iron(II) carbonate</a> being precipitated out. In excess carbon dioxide this forms the slightly soluble bicarbonate, which occurs commonly in groundwater, but it oxidises quickly in air to form <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide" title="Iron(III) oxide">iron(III) oxide</a> that accounts for the brown deposits present in a sizeable number of streams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1091_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1091-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordination_compounds">Coordination compounds</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2-isomers-of-ferrioxalate.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="227" data-file-width="391" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/2-isomers-of-ferrioxalate.svg/300px-2-isomers-of-ferrioxalate.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/2-isomers-of-ferrioxalate.svg/450px-2-isomers-of-ferrioxalate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/2-isomers-of-ferrioxalate.svg/600px-2-isomers-of-ferrioxalate.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2-isomers-of-ferrioxalate.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The two <a href="/wiki/Enantiomer" title="Enantiomer">enantiomorphs</a> of the ferrioxalate ion</div></div></div>
<p>Many coordination compounds of iron are known.  A typical six-coordinate anion is hexachloroferrate(III), [FeCl<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3−</sup>, found in the mixed <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salt</a> <a href="/wiki/Tetrakis(methylammonium)_hexachloroferrate(III)_chloride" title="Tetrakis(methylammonium) hexachloroferrate(III) chloride">tetrakis(methylammonium) hexachloroferrate(III) chloride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>  Complexes with multiple bidentate ligands have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geometric_isomer" title="Geometric isomer">geometric isomers</a>.  For example, the <i>trans</i>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlorohydridobis(bis-1,2-(diphenylphosphino)ethane)iron(II)" title="Chlorohydridobis(bis-1,2-(diphenylphosphino)ethane)iron(II)">chlorohydridobis(bis-1,2-(diphenylphosphino)ethane)iron(II)</a> complex is used as a starting material for compounds with the Fe(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dppe" title="Dppe">dppe</a>)<sub>2</sub> <a href="/wiki/Moiety_(chemistry)" title="Moiety (chemistry)">moiety</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>  The ferrioxalate ion with three <a href="/wiki/Oxalate" title="Oxalate">oxalate</a> ligands (shown at right) displays <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helical_chirality" title="Helical chirality">helical chirality</a> with its two non-superposable geometries labelled <i>Λ</i> (lambda) for the left-handed screw axis and <i>Δ</i> (delta) for the right-handed screw axis, in line with IUPAC conventions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1088_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1088-56">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Potassium_ferrioxalate" title="Potassium ferrioxalate">Potassium ferrioxalate</a> is used in chemical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Actinometry" title="Actinometry">actinometry</a> and along with its <a href="/wiki/Sodium_ferrioxalate" title="Sodium ferrioxalate">sodium salt</a> undergoes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photoreduction" title="Photoreduction">photoreduction</a> applied in old-style photographic processes.  The <a href="/wiki/Hydrate" title="Hydrate">dihydrate</a> of <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_oxalate" title="Iron(II) oxalate">iron(II) oxalate</a> has a <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymeric</a> structure with co-planar oxalate ions bridging between iron centres with the water of crystallisation located forming the caps of each octahedron, as illustrated below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fe(C2O4)(H2O)2-chain-from-xtal-2008-CM-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="507" data-file-width="2150" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Fe%28C2O4%29%28H2O%292-chain-from-xtal-2008-CM-3D-balls.png/500px-Fe%28C2O4%29%28H2O%292-chain-from-xtal-2008-CM-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Fe%28C2O4%29%28H2O%292-chain-from-xtal-2008-CM-3D-balls.png/750px-Fe%28C2O4%29%28H2O%292-chain-from-xtal-2008-CM-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Fe%28C2O4%29%28H2O%292-chain-from-xtal-2008-CM-3D-balls.png/1000px-Fe%28C2O4%29%28H2O%292-chain-from-xtal-2008-CM-3D-balls.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fe(C2O4)(H2O)2-chain-from-xtal-2008-CM-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ball-and-stick model of a chain in the crystal structure of iron(II) oxalate dihydrate</div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prussian_blue.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="1914" height="48" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Prussian_blue.jpg/220px-Prussian_blue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Prussian_blue.jpg/330px-Prussian_blue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Prussian_blue.jpg/440px-Prussian_blue.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prussian_blue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Prussian_blue" title="Prussian blue">Prussian blue</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Prussian_blue" title="Prussian blue">Prussian blue</a>, Fe<sub>4</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>3</sub>, is the most famous of the cyanide complexes of iron.  Its formation can be used as a simple wet chemistry test to distinguish between aqueous solutions of Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup> as they react (respectively) with <a href="/wiki/Potassium_ferricyanide" title="Potassium ferricyanide">potassium ferricyanide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_ferrocyanide" title="Potassium ferrocyanide">potassium ferrocyanide</a> to form Prussian blue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollemanAF_50-5"><a href="#cite_note-HollemanAF-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pentaaqua(thiocyanato)iron(III)_chloride.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Pentaaqua%28thiocyanato%29iron%28III%29_chloride.jpg/150px-Pentaaqua%28thiocyanato%29iron%28III%29_chloride.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Pentaaqua%28thiocyanato%29iron%28III%29_chloride.jpg/225px-Pentaaqua%28thiocyanato%29iron%28III%29_chloride.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Pentaaqua%28thiocyanato%29iron%28III%29_chloride.jpg/300px-Pentaaqua%28thiocyanato%29iron%28III%29_chloride.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pentaaqua(thiocyanato)iron(III)_chloride.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Blood-red positive thiocyanate test for iron(III)</div></div></div>
<p>Iron(III) complexes are quite similar to those of <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a>(III) with the exception of iron(III)'s preference for <i>O</i>-donor instead of <i>N</i>-donor ligands. The latter tend to be rather more unstable than iron(II) complexes and often dissociate in water. Many Fe–O complexes show intense colors and are used as tests for <a href="/wiki/Phenol" title="Phenol">phenols</a> or <a href="/wiki/Enol" title="Enol">enols</a>. For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Ferric_chloride_test" title="Ferric chloride test">ferric chloride test</a>, used to determine the presence of phenols, <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_chloride" title="Iron(III) chloride">iron(III) chloride</a> reacts with a phenol to form a deep violet complex:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1088_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1088-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>3 ArOH + FeCl<sub>3</sub> → Fe(OAr)<sub>3</sub> + 3 HCl (Ar = <a href="/wiki/Aryl" title="Aryl">aryl</a>)</dd></dl>
<p>Among the halide and pseudohalide complexes, fluoro complexes of iron(III) are the most stable, with the colorless [FeF<sub>5</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)]<sup>2−</sup> being the most stable in aqueous solution. Chloro complexes are less stable and favor tetrahedral coordination as in [FeCl<sub>4</sub>]<sup>−</sup>; [FeBr<sub>4</sub>]<sup>−</sup> and [FeI<sub>4</sub>]<sup>−</sup> are reduced easily to iron(II). <a href="/wiki/Thiocyanate" title="Thiocyanate">Thiocyanate</a> is a common test for the presence of iron(III) as it forms the blood-red [Fe(SCN)(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>5</sub>]<sup>2+</sup>. Like manganese(II), most iron(III) complexes are high-spin, the exceptions being those with ligands that are high in the <a href="/wiki/Spectrochemical_series" title="Spectrochemical series">spectrochemical series</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanide</a>. An example of a low-spin iron(III) complex is [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3−</sup>. The cyanide ligands may easily be detached in [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3−</sup>, and hence this complex is poisonous, unlike the iron(II) complex [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4−</sup> found in Prussian blue,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1088_56-4"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1088-56">[56]</a></sup> which does not release <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">hydrogen cyanide</a> except when dilute acids are added.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1091_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1091-57">[57]</a></sup> Iron shows a great variety of electronic <a href="/wiki/Spin_states_(d_electrons)" title="Spin states (d electrons)">spin states</a>, including every possible spin quantum number value for a d-block element from 0 (diamagnetic) to ​<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>5</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> (5 unpaired electrons). This value is always half the number of unpaired electrons. Complexes with zero to two unpaired electrons are considered low-spin and those with four or five are considered high-spin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1079_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1079-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Iron(II) complexes are less stable than iron(III) complexes but the preference for <i>O</i>-donor ligands is less marked, so that for example [Fe(NH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>2+</sup> is known while [Fe(NH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3+</sup> is not. They have a tendency to be oxidized to iron(III) but this can be moderated by low pH and the specific ligands used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1091_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1091-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organometallic_compounds">Organometallic compounds</span></h3>
