<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Photo_of_Ferrocene_(powdered).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="573" data-file-width="906" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Photo_of_Ferrocene_%28powdered%29.JPG/200px-Photo_of_Ferrocene_%28powdered%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Photo_of_Ferrocene_%28powdered%29.JPG/300px-Photo_of_Ferrocene_%28powdered%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Photo_of_Ferrocene_%28powdered%29.JPG/400px-Photo_of_Ferrocene_%28powdered%29.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Photo_of_Ferrocene_(powdered).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Powdered ferrocene</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Organoiron_chemistry" title="Organoiron chemistry">Organoiron chemistry</a></div>
<p>Cyanide complexes are technically <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organometallic_compound" title="Organometallic compound">organometallic</a> but more important are <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbonyl</a> complexes and <a href="/wiki/Sandwich_compound" title="Sandwich compound">sandwich</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half-sandwich_compound" title="Half-sandwich compound">half-sandwich compounds</a>.  The premier iron(0) compound is <a href="/wiki/Iron_pentacarbonyl" title="Iron pentacarbonyl">iron pentacarbonyl</a>, Fe(CO)<sub>5</sub>, which is used to produce <a href="/wiki/Carbonyl_iron" title="Carbonyl iron">carbonyl iron</a> powder, a highly reactive form of metallic iron. Thermolysis of iron pentacarbonyl gives the trinuclear cluster, <a href="/wiki/Triiron_dodecacarbonyl" title="Triiron dodecacarbonyl">triiron dodecacarbonyl</a>. Collman's reagent, <a href="/wiki/Disodium_tetracarbonylferrate" title="Disodium tetracarbonylferrate">disodium tetracarbonylferrate</a>, is a useful reagent for organic chemistry; it contains iron in the −2 oxidation state. <a href="/wiki/Cyclopentadienyliron_dicarbonyl_dimer" title="Cyclopentadienyliron dicarbonyl dimer">Cyclopentadienyliron dicarbonyl dimer</a> contains iron in the rare +1 oxidation state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ferrocene" title="Ferrocene">Ferrocene</a> was an extremely important compound in the early history of the branch of <a href="/wiki/Organometallic_chemistry" title="Organometallic chemistry">organometallic chemistry</a>, and to this day iron is still one of the most important metals in this field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1104_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1104-64">[64]</a></sup> It was first synthesised in 1951 during an attempt to prepare the <a href="/wiki/Fulvalene" title="Fulvalene">fulvalene</a> (C<sub>10</sub>H<sub>8</sub>) by oxidative dimerization of <a href="/wiki/Cyclopentadiene" title="Cyclopentadiene">cyclopentadiene</a>; the resultant product was found to have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_formula" title="Molecular formula">molecular formula</a> C<sub>10</sub>H<sub>10</sub>Fe and reported to exhibit "remarkable stability".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The discovery sparked substantial interest in the field of organometallic chemistry,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Federman_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Federman-67">[67]</a></sup> in part because the structure proposed by Pauson and Kealy (shown at right) was inconsistent with then-existing bonding models and did not explain its unexpected stability. Consequently, the initial challenge was to definitively determine the structure of ferrocene in the hope that its bonding and properties would then be understood. The shockingly novel sandwich structure, [Fe(η<sup>5</sup>-C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>],<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman-66">[66]</a></sup> was deduced and reported independently by three groups in 1952: <a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns_Woodward" title="Robert Burns Woodward">Robert Burns Woodward</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Wilkinson" title="Geoffrey Wilkinson">Geoffrey Wilkinson</a> investigated the reactivity in order to determine the structure<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> and demonstrated that ferrocene undergoes similar reactions to a typical aromatic molecule (such as <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Otto_Fischer" title="Ernst Otto Fischer">Ernst Otto Fischer</a> deduced the sandwich structure and also began synthesising other <a href="/wiki/Metallocene" title="Metallocene">metallocenes</a> including <a href="/wiki/Cobaltocene" title="Cobaltocene">cobaltocene</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Eiland and Pepinsky provided <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray crystallographic</a> confirmation of the sandwich structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Applying <a href="/wiki/Valence_bond_theory" title="Valence bond theory">valence bond theory</a> to ferrocene by considering an Fe<sup>2+</sup> centre and two cyclopentadienide anions (C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub><sup>−</sup>), which are known to be <a href="/wiki/Aromaticity" title="Aromaticity">aromatic</a> according to <a href="/wiki/H%C3%BCckel%27s_rule" title="Hückel's rule">Hückel's rule</a> and hence highly stable, allowed correct prediction of the geometry of the molecule. Once <a href="/wiki/Molecular_orbital_theory" title="Molecular orbital theory">molecular orbital theory</a> was successfully applied and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dewar-Chatt-Duncanson_model" title="Dewar-Chatt-Duncanson model">Dewar-Chatt-Duncanson model</a> proposed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> the reasons for ferrocene's remarkable stability became clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Ferrocene was not the first organometallic compound known – <a href="/wiki/Zeise%27s_salt" title="Zeise's salt">Zeise's salt</a>, K[PtCl<sub>3</sub>(C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>)]·H<sub>2</sub>O was reported in 1831<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Mond" title="Ludwig Mond">Mond's</a> discovery of Ni(CO)<sub>4</sub> occurred in 1888,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> but it was ferrocene's discovery that began <a href="/wiki/Organometallic_chemistry" title="Organometallic chemistry">organometallic chemistry</a> as a separate area of chemistry.  It was so important that Wilkinson and Fischer shared the 1973 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize</a> for Chemistry "for their pioneering work, performed independently, on the chemistry of the organometallic, so called <a href="/wiki/Sandwich_compound" title="Sandwich compound">sandwich compounds</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Ferrocene itself can be used as the backbone of a ligand, e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1,1%27-bis(diphenylphosphino)ferrocene" title="1,1'-bis(diphenylphosphino)ferrocene">1,1'-bis(diphenylphosphino)ferrocene</a> (dppf). Ferrocene can itself be oxidized to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrocenium" title="Ferrocenium">ferrocenium</a> cation (Fc<sup>+</sup>); the ferrocene/ferrocenium couple is often used as a reference in electrochemistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Federman_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Federman-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Metallocene" title="Metallocene">Metallocenes</a> like ferrocene can be prepared by reaction of <a href="/wiki/Dicyclopentadiene" title="Dicyclopentadiene">freshly-cracked</a> cyclopentadiene with <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_chloride" title="Iron(II) chloride">iron(II) chloride</a> and base.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>  It is an <a href="/wiki/Aromaticity" title="Aromaticity">aromatic substance</a> and undergoes <a href="/wiki/Substitution_reaction" title="Substitution reaction">substitution reactions</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Addition_reaction" title="Addition reaction">addition reactions</a> on the cyclopentadienyl ligands.  For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friedel-Crafts_reaction#Friedel-Crafts_acylation" title="Friedel-Crafts reaction">Friedel-Crafts acylation</a> of ferrocene with <a href="/wiki/Acetic_anhydride" title="Acetic anhydride">acetic anhydride</a> yields <a href="/wiki/Acetylferrocene" title="Acetylferrocene">acetylferrocene</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> just as acylation of benzene yields <a href="/wiki/Acetophenone" title="Acetophenone">acetophenone</a> under similar conditions.
</p>
<dl><dd><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Synthesis_of_acetylferrocene_from_dicyclopentadiene.png"><img alt="Synthesis of acetylferrocene from dicyclopentadiene.png" data-file-height="283" data-file-width="1622" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Synthesis_of_acetylferrocene_from_dicyclopentadiene.png/800px-Synthesis_of_acetylferrocene_from_dicyclopentadiene.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Synthesis_of_acetylferrocene_from_dicyclopentadiene.png/1200px-Synthesis_of_acetylferrocene_from_dicyclopentadiene.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Synthesis_of_acetylferrocene_from_dicyclopentadiene.png/1600px-Synthesis_of_acetylferrocene_from_dicyclopentadiene.png 2x" width="800"/></a></dd></dl>
<p>Iron-centered organometallic species are used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalysts</a>.  The <a href="/wiki/Kn%C3%B6lker_complex" title="Knölker complex">Knölker complex</a>, for example, is a <a href="/wiki/Transfer_hydrogenation" title="Transfer hydrogenation">transfer hydrogenation</a> catalyst for <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">ketones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:141px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beowulf_-_iren.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="75" data-file-width="139" height="75" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/56/Beowulf_-_iren.jpg" width="139"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beowulf_-_iren.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"iren," an Old English word for 'iron'</div></div></div>
<p>As iron has been in use for such a long time, it has many different names in different languages. The source of its chemical symbol <i>Fe</i> is the Latin word <i>ferrum</i>, and its descendants are the names of the element in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_language" title="Romance language">Romance languages</a> (for example, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> <i>fer</i>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a> <i>hierro</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a> <i>ferro</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanderkrogt_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-vanderkrogt-81">[81]</a></sup> The word <i>ferrum</i> itself possibly comes from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semitic_language" title="Semitic language">Semitic languages</a>, via <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_language" title="Etruscan language">Etruscan</a>, from a root that also gave rise to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_English_language" title="Old English language">Old English</a> <i>bræs</i> "<a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brass</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> The English word <i>iron</i> derives ultimately from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic</a> <i>*isarnan</i>, which is also the source of the German name <i>Eisen</i>. It was most likely borrowed from <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic</a> <i>*isarnon</i>, which ultimately comes from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" title="Proto-Indo-European">Proto-Indo-European</a> <i>*is-(e)ro-</i> "powerful, holy" and finally <i>*eis</i> "strong", referencing iron's strength as a metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Kluge relates <i>*isarnon</i> to Illyric and Latin <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wrath" title="Wrath">ira</a></i>, 'wrath').<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balto-Slavic_language" title="Balto-Slavic language">Balto-Slavic</a> names for iron (for example, <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> <i>железо</i> [<i>zhelezo</i>], <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a> <i>żelazo</i>, <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a> <i>geležis</i>) are the only ones to come directly from the Proto-Indo-European <i>*g<sup>h</sup>elg<sup>h</sup>-</i> "iron".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> In many of these languages, the word for <i>iron</i> may also be used to denote other objects made of iron or steel, or figuratively because of the hardness and strength of the metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> <i>tiě</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese" title="Traditional Chinese">traditional</a> 鐵; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese" title="Simplified Chinese">simplified</a> 铁) derives from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Sino-Tibetan_language" title="Proto-Sino-Tibetan language">Proto-Sino-Tibetan</a> <i>*hliek</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> and was borrowed into <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> as 鉄 <i>tetsu</i>, which also has the native reading <i>kurogane</i> "black metal" (similar to how iron is referenced in the English word <a href="/wiki/Blacksmith" title="Blacksmith">blacksmith</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_ferrous_metallurgy" title="History of ferrous metallurgy">History of ferrous metallurgy</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wrought_iron">Wrought iron</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_iron_production" title="Ancient iron production">Ancient iron production</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:82px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars_symbol.svg"><img alt="A circle, with a short, simple arrow shape extending diagonally upwards and rightwards from its edge" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Mars_symbol.svg/80px-Mars_symbol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Mars_symbol.svg/120px-Mars_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Mars_symbol.svg/160px-Mars_symbol.svg.png 2x" width="80"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mars_symbol.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The symbol for <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> has been used since antiquity to represent iron.</div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:QtubIronPillar.JPG"><img alt="An pillar, slightly fluted, with some ornamentation at its top. It is black, slightly weathered to a dark brown near the base. It is around 7 meters (23 feet) tall. It stands upon a raised circular base of stone, and is surrounded by a short, square fence." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1347" data-file-width="1000" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/QtubIronPillar.JPG/220px-QtubIronPillar.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/QtubIronPillar.JPG/330px-QtubIronPillar.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/QtubIronPillar.JPG/440px-QtubIronPillar.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:QtubIronPillar.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Iron_pillar_of_Delhi" title="Iron pillar of Delhi">iron pillar of Delhi</a> is an example of the iron extraction and processing methodologies of early India.</div></div></div>
<p>Iron is one of the elements undoubtedly known to the ancient world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks19684_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks19684-88">[88]</a></sup> It has been worked, or <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">wrought</a>, for millennia.  However, iron objects of great age are much rarer than objects made of gold or silver due to the ease with which iron corrodes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks196829_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks196829-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meteorite_iron_harpoon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1759" data-file-width="2363" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Meteorite_iron_harpoon.jpg/220px-Meteorite_iron_harpoon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Meteorite_iron_harpoon.jpg/330px-Meteorite_iron_harpoon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Meteorite_iron_harpoon.jpg/440px-Meteorite_iron_harpoon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meteorite_iron_harpoon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iron harpoon head from <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>. The iron edge covers a <a href="/wiki/Narwhal" title="Narwhal">narwhal</a>tusk harpoon using meteorite iron from the <a href="/wiki/Cape_York_meteorite" title="Cape York meteorite">Cape York meteorite</a>, one of the largest iron meteorites known.</div></div></div>
<p>Beads made from <a href="/wiki/Meteoric_iron" title="Meteoric iron">meteoric iron</a> in 3500 BC or earlier were found in Gerzah, Egypt by G. A. Wainwright.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks196831_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks196831-90">[90]</a></sup> The beads contain 7.5% nickel, which is a signature of meteoric origin since iron found in the Earth's crust generally has only minuscule nickel impurities. Meteoric iron was highly regarded due to its origin in the heavens and was often used to forge weapons and tools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks196831_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks196831-90">[90]</a></sup> For example, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tutankhamun%27s_iron_dagger_blade" title="Tutankhamun's iron dagger blade">dagger</a> made of meteoric iron was found in the tomb of <a href="/wiki/Tutankhamun" title="Tutankhamun">Tutankhamun</a>, containing similar proportions of iron, cobalt, and nickel to a meteorite discovered in the area, deposited by an ancient meteor shower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bjorkman_1973_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bjorkman_1973-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Comelli_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Comelli-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-walshx_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-walshx-93">[93]</a></sup> Items that were likely made of iron by Egyptians date from 3000 to 2500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks196829_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks196829-89">[89]</a></sup> Meteoritic iron is comparably soft and ductile and easily forged by <a href="/wiki/Work_hardening" title="Work hardening">cold working</a> but may get brittle when heated because of the <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first iron production started in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Bronze_Age" title="Middle Bronze Age">Middle Bronze Age</a> but it took several centuries before iron displaced bronze. Samples of <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelted</a> iron from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asmar" title="Asmar">Asmar</a>, Mesopotamia and Tall Chagar Bazaar in northern Syria were made sometime between 3000 and 2700 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks196832_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks196832-95">[95]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittites</a> established an empire in north-central <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> around 1600 BC. They appear to be the first to understand the production of iron from its ores and regard it highly in their society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittites</a> began to smelt iron between 1500 and 1200 BC and the practice spread to the rest of the Near East after their empire fell in 1180 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks196832_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks196832-95">[95]</a></sup> The subsequent period is called the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>.
</p><p>Artifacts of smelted iron are found in <a href="/wiki/History_of_metallurgy_in_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="History of metallurgy in the Indian subcontinent">India</a> dating from 1800 to 1200 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tewari_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tewari-97">[97]</a></sup> and in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> from about 1500 BC (suggesting smelting in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Alleged references (compare <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_metallurgy_in_South_Asia" title="History of metallurgy in South Asia">history of metallurgy in South Asia</a>) to iron in the Indian <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> have been used for claims of a very early usage of iron in India respectively to date the texts as such. The <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">rigveda</a> term <i>ayas</i> (metal) probably refers to copper and bronze, while iron or <i>śyāma ayas</i>, literally "black metal", first is mentioned in the post-rigvedic <a href="/wiki/Atharvaveda" title="Atharvaveda">Atharvaveda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some archaeological evidence suggests iron was smelted in <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> and southeast Africa as early as the eighth century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Iron working was introduced to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greece</a> in the late 11th century BC, from which it spread quickly throughout Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>The spread of ironworking in Central and Western Europe is associated with <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> expansion. According to <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, iron use was common in the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks196831_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks196831-90">[90]</a></sup> The annual iron output of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> is estimated at <span style="white-space:nowrap">84750</span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tonnes" title="Tonnes">t</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> while the similarly populous and contemporary Han China produced around <span style="white-space:nowrap">5000</span> t.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> In China, iron only appears circa 700–500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Iron smelting may have been introduced into China through Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pigott2_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-pigott2-106">[106]</a></sup> The earliest evidence of the use of a <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a> in China dates to the 1st century AD,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golas1999_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Golas1999-107">[107]</a></sup> and cupola furnaces were used as early as the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a> (403–221 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pigott_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-pigott-108">[108]</a></sup> Usage of the blast and cupola furnace remained widespread during the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang Dynasties</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Coming_of_the_Ages_of_Steel_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Coming_of_the_Ages_of_Steel-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Industrial Revolution in Britain, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cort" title="Henry Cort">Henry Cort</a> began refining iron from <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a> to <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">wrought iron</a> (or bar iron) using innovative production systems. In 1783 he patented the <a href="/wiki/Puddling_(metallurgy)" title="Puddling (metallurgy)">puddling process</a> for refining iron ore. It was later improved by others, including <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Hall_(metallurgist)" title="Joseph Hall (metallurgist)">Joseph Hall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cast_iron">Cast iron</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">Cast iron</a> was first produced in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> during 5th century BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> but was hardly in Europe until the medieval period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biddle_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Biddle-113">[113]</a></sup> The earliest <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a> artifacts were discovered by archaeologists in what is now modern <a href="/wiki/Luhe_County" title="Luhe County">Luhe County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a> in China. Cast iron was used in <a href="/wiki/History_of_China#Ancient_China" title="History of China">ancient China</a> for warfare, agriculture, and architecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wagner_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wagner-114">[114]</a></sup> During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval" title="Medieval">medieval</a> period, means were found in Europe of producing wrought iron from cast iron (in this context known as <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a>) using <a href="/wiki/Finery_forge" title="Finery forge">finery forges</a>. For all these processes, <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> was required as fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1072_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1072-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Philipp_Jakob_Loutherbourg_d._J._002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1009" data-file-width="1571" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Philipp_Jakob_Loutherbourg_d._J._002.jpg/220px-Philipp_Jakob_Loutherbourg_d._J._002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Philipp_Jakob_Loutherbourg_d._J._002.jpg/330px-Philipp_Jakob_Loutherbourg_d._J._002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Philipp_Jakob_Loutherbourg_d._J._002.jpg/440px-Philipp_Jakob_Loutherbourg_d._J._002.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Philipp_Jakob_Loutherbourg_d._J._002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Coalbrookdale_by_Night" title="Coalbrookdale by Night">Coalbrookdale by Night</a></i>, 1801. Blast furnaces light the iron making town of <a href="/wiki/Coalbrookdale" title="Coalbrookdale">Coalbrookdale</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Medieval <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blast_furnaces" title="Blast furnaces">blast furnaces</a> were about 10 feet (3.0 m) tall and made of fireproof brick; forced air was usually provided by hand-operated bellows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biddle_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Biddle-113">[113]</a></sup> Modern blast furnaces have grown much bigger, with hearths fourteen meters in diameter that allow them to produce thousands of tons of iron each day, but essentially operate in much the same way as they did during medieval times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1072_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1072-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1709, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Darby_I" title="Abraham Darby I">Abraham Darby I</a> established a <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a>-fired blast furnace to produce cast iron, replacing charcoal, although continuing to use blast furnaces. The ensuing availability of inexpensive iron was one of the factors leading to the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. Toward the end of the 18th century, cast iron began to replace wrought iron for certain purposes, because it was cheaper. Carbon content in iron was not implicated as the reason for the differences in properties of wrought iron, cast iron, and steel until the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks196832_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks196832-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since iron was becoming cheaper and more plentiful, it also became a major structural material following the building of the innovative <a href="/wiki/The_Iron_Bridge" title="The Iron Bridge">first iron bridge</a> in 1778. This bridge still stands today as a monument to the role iron played in the Industrial Revolution. Following this, iron was used in rails, boats, ships, aqueducts, and buildings, as well as in iron cylinders in <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1072_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1072-115">[115]</a></sup> Railways have been central to the formation of modernity and ideas of progress<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> and various languages (e.g. French, Spanish, Italian and German) refer to railways as <i>iron road</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steel">Steel</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Steelmaking" title="Steelmaking">Steelmaking</a></div>
<p>Steel (with smaller carbon content than pig iron but more than wrought iron) was first produced in antiquity by using a <a href="/wiki/Bloomery" title="Bloomery">bloomery</a>. Blacksmiths in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luristan" title="Luristan">Luristan</a> in western Persia were making good steel by 1000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeeks196832_95-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeeks196832-95">[95]</a></sup> Then improved versions, <a href="/wiki/Wootz_steel" title="Wootz steel">Wootz steel</a> by India and <a href="/wiki/Damascus_steel" title="Damascus steel">Damascus steel</a> were developed around 300 BC and AD 500 respectively. These methods were specialized, and so steel did not become a major commodity until the 1850s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>New methods of producing it by <a href="/wiki/Carburizing" title="Carburizing">carburizing</a> bars of iron in the <a href="/wiki/Cementation_process" title="Cementation process">cementation process</a> were devised in the 17th century. In the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, new methods of producing bar iron without charcoal were devised and these were later applied to produce steel. In the late 1850s, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Bessemer" title="Henry Bessemer">Henry Bessemer</a> invented a new steelmaking process, involving blowing air through molten pig iron, to produce mild steel. This made steel much more economical, thereby leading to wrought iron no longer being produced in large quantities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundations_of_modern_chemistry">Foundations of modern chemistry</span></h3>
<p>In 1774, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> used the reaction of water steam with metallic iron inside an incandescent iron tube to produce <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> in his experiments leading to the demonstration of the <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_mass" title="Conservation of mass">conservation of mass</a>, which was instrumental in changing chemistry from a qualitative science to a quantitative one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbolic_role">Symbolic role</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eisernes_Kreuz_(1914).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="398" data-file-width="352" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Eisernes_Kreuz_%281914%29.jpg/220px-Eisernes_Kreuz_%281914%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Eisernes_Kreuz_%281914%29.jpg/330px-Eisernes_Kreuz_%281914%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1f/Eisernes_Kreuz_%281914%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eisernes_Kreuz_(1914).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<i>Gold gab ich für Eisen</i>" – "I gave gold for iron". <a href="/wiki/German_Americans" title="German Americans">German-American</a> brooch from WWI.</div></div></div>
<p>Iron plays a certain role in mythology and has found various usage <a href="/wiki/Iron_(metaphor)" title="Iron (metaphor)">as a metaphor</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Iron_in_folklore" title="Iron in folklore">folklore</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> poet <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Works_and_Days" title="Works and Days">Works and Days</a></i> (lines 109–201) lists different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ages_of_man" title="Ages of man">ages of man</a> named after metals like gold, silver, bronze and iron to account for successive ages of humanity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The Iron Age was closely related with Rome, and in Ovid's <i>Metamorphoses</i>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Virtues, in despair, quit the earth; and the depravity of man becomes universal and complete. Hard steel succeeded then.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Ovid, <a href="/wiki/Metamorphoses" title="Metamorphoses">Metamorphoses</a>, Book I, Iron age, line 160 ff</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>An example of the importance of iron's symbolic role may be found in the <a href="/wiki/German_Campaign_of_1813" title="German Campaign of 1813">German Campaign of 1813</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frederick_William_III" title="Frederick William III">Frederick William III</a> commissioned then the first <a href="/wiki/Iron_Cross" title="Iron Cross">Iron Cross</a> as military decoration. <a href="/wiki/Berlin_iron_jewellery" title="Berlin iron jewellery">Berlin iron jewellery</a> reached its peak production between 1813 and 1815, when the Prussian <a href="/wiki/Royal_family" title="Royal family">royal family</a> urged citizens to donate gold and silver jewellery for military funding. The inscription <i>Gold gab ich für Eisen</i> (I gave gold for iron) was used as well in later war efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_of_metallic_iron"><span id="Industrial_production"></span><span id="Production_of_metallic_iron">Production of metallic iron</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iron_powder.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Iron_powder.JPG/220px-Iron_powder.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Iron_powder.JPG/330px-Iron_powder.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Iron_powder.JPG/440px-Iron_powder.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iron_powder.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Iron_powder" title="Iron powder">Iron powder</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_routes">Industrial routes</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">Iron ore</a></div>
<p>The production of iron or steel is a process consisting of two main stages. In the first stage pig iron is produced in a blast furnace. Alternatively, it may be directly reduced. In the second stage, pig iron is converted to wrought iron, steel, or cast iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1073_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1073-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Fining_and_Blast_Furnace.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="794" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Chinese_Fining_and_Blast_Furnace.jpg/220px-Chinese_Fining_and_Blast_Furnace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Chinese_Fining_and_Blast_Furnace.jpg/330px-Chinese_Fining_and_Blast_Furnace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Chinese_Fining_and_Blast_Furnace.jpg/440px-Chinese_Fining_and_Blast_Furnace.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Fining_and_Blast_Furnace.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The fining process of smelting <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron ore</a> to make wrought iron from pig iron, with the right illustration displaying men working a blast furnace, from the <i>Tiangong Kaiwu</i> encyclopedia, published in 1637 by <a href="/wiki/Song_Yingxing" title="Song Yingxing">Song Yingxing</a>.</div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iron-Making.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1914" data-file-width="2524" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Iron-Making.jpg/220px-Iron-Making.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Iron-Making.jpg/330px-Iron-Making.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Iron-Making.jpg/440px-Iron-Making.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iron-Making.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>How iron was extracted in the 19th century</div></div></div>
<p>For a few limited purposes when it is needed, pure iron is produced in the laboratory in small quantities by reducing the pure oxide or hydroxide with hydrogen, or forming iron pentacarbonyl and heating it to 250 °C so that it decomposes to form pure iron powder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1071_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1071-42">[42]</a></sup> Another method is electrolysis of ferrous chloride onto an iron cathode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Blast_furnace_processing">Blast furnace processing</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">Blast furnace</a></div>
<p>Industrial iron production starts with iron ores, principally <a href="/wiki/Hematite" title="Hematite">hematite</a>, which has a nominal formula Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, and <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a>, with the formula Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>.  These ores are reduced to the metal in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbothermic" title="Carbothermic">carbothermic</a> reaction, i.e. by treatment with carbon.  The conversion is typically conducted in a blast furnace at temperatures of about 2000 °C. Carbon is provided in the form of coke.  The process also contains a <a href="/wiki/Flux_(metallurgy)" title="Flux (metallurgy)">flux</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>, which is used to remove silicaceous minerals in the ore, which would otherwise clog the furnace.  The coke and limestone are fed into the top of the furnace, while a massive blast of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">air</a> heated to 900 °C, about 4 tons per ton of iron,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biddle_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-Biddle-113">[113]</a></sup> is forced into the furnace at the bottom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1073_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1073-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the furnace, the coke reacts with oxygen in the air blast to produce <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1073_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1073-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 C + O<sub>2</sub> → 2 CO</dd></dl>
<p>The carbon monoxide reduces the iron ore (in the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_equation" title="Chemical equation">chemical equation</a> below, hematite) to molten iron, becoming <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> in the process:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1073_122-3"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1073-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 3 CO → 2 Fe + 3 CO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Some iron in the high-temperature lower region of the furnace reacts directly with the coke:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1073_122-4"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1073-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 3 C → 4 Fe + 3 CO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>The flux present to melt impurities in the ore is principally <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> (<a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Dolomite" title="Dolomite">dolomite</a> (calcium-magnesium carbonate). Other specialized fluxes are used depending on the details of the ore. In the heat of the furnace the limestone flux decomposes to <a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">calcium oxide</a> (also known as quicklime):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1073_122-5"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1073-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>CaCO<sub>3</sub> → CaO + CO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Then calcium oxide combines with silicon dioxide to form a liquid <a href="/wiki/Slag" title="Slag">slag</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1073_122-6"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1073-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>CaO + SiO<sub>2</sub> → CaSiO<sub>3</sub></dd></dl>
<p>The slag melts in the heat of the furnace. In the bottom of the furnace, the molten slag floats on top of the denser molten iron, and apertures in the side of the furnace are opened to run off the iron and the slag separately. The iron, once cooled, is called pig iron, while the slag can be used as a material in <a href="/wiki/Road" title="Road">road</a> construction or to improve mineral-poor soils for <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biddle_113-3"><a href="#cite_note-Biddle-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LightningVolt_Iron_Ore_Pellets.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="354" data-file-width="512" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/LightningVolt_Iron_Ore_Pellets.jpg/220px-LightningVolt_Iron_Ore_Pellets.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/LightningVolt_Iron_Ore_Pellets.jpg/330px-LightningVolt_Iron_Ore_Pellets.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/LightningVolt_Iron_Ore_Pellets.jpg/440px-LightningVolt_Iron_Ore_Pellets.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LightningVolt_Iron_Ore_Pellets.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This heap of iron ore pellets will be used in steel production.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_iron_reduction">Direct iron reduction</span></h4>
<p>Owing to environmental concerns, alternative methods of processing iron have been developed. "<a href="/wiki/Direct_reduced_iron" title="Direct reduced iron">Direct iron reduction</a>" <a href="/wiki/Sponge_iron_reaction" title="Sponge iron reaction">reduces iron ore</a> to a ferrous lump called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sponge_iron" title="Sponge iron">"sponge" iron</a> or "direct" iron that is suitable for steelmaking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biddle_113-4"><a href="#cite_note-Biddle-113">[113]</a></sup> Two main reactions comprise the direct reduction process:
</p><p>Natural gas is partially oxidized (with heat and a catalyst):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biddle_113-5"><a href="#cite_note-Biddle-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 CH<sub>4</sub> + O<sub>2</sub> → 2 CO + 4 H<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>These gases are then treated with iron ore in a furnace, producing solid sponge iron:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biddle_113-6"><a href="#cite_note-Biddle-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + CO + 2 H<sub>2</sub> → 2 Fe + CO<sub>2</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">Silica</a> is removed by adding a <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> flux as described above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biddle_113-7"><a href="#cite_note-Biddle-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermite">Thermite</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thermite" title="Thermite">Thermite</a></div>
<p>Iron is a byproduct of burning a mixture of aluminum powder and rust powder.
</p>
<dl><dd>Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 2 Al → 2 Fe + Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub></dd></dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_processes">Further processes</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Steelmaking" title="Steelmaking">Steelmaking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ironworks" title="Ironworks">Ironworks</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Melted_raw-iron.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Melted_raw-iron.jpg/220px-Melted_raw-iron.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Melted_raw-iron.jpg/330px-Melted_raw-iron.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Melted_raw-iron.jpg/440px-Melted_raw-iron.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Melted_raw-iron.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pot of molten iron being used to make steel</div></div></div>
<p>Pig iron is not pure iron, but has 4–5% carbon dissolved in it with small amounts of other impurities like sulfur, magnesium, phosphorus and manganese. As the carbon is the major impurity, the iron (pig iron) becomes brittle and hard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1073_122-7"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1073-122">[122]</a></sup> Removing the other impurities results in cast iron, which is used to cast articles in <a href="/wiki/Foundry" title="Foundry">foundries</a> such as stoves, pipes, radiators, lamp-posts and rails.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1073_122-8"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1073-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alternatively pig iron may be made into steel (with up to about 2% carbon) or wrought iron (commercially pure iron). Various processes have been used for this, including <a href="/wiki/Finery_forge" title="Finery forge">finery forges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puddling_(metallurgy)" title="Puddling (metallurgy)">puddling</a> furnaces, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bessemer_converter" title="Bessemer converter">Bessemer converters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Open_hearth_furnace" title="Open hearth furnace">open hearth furnaces</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basic_oxygen_furnace" title="Basic oxygen furnace">basic oxygen furnaces</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc_furnace" title="Electric arc furnace">electric arc furnaces</a>. In all cases, the objective is to oxidize some or all of the carbon, together with other impurities. On the other hand, other metals may be added to make alloy steels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1072_115-3"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1072-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Annealing_(metallurgy)" title="Annealing (metallurgy)">Annealing</a> involves the heating of a piece of steel to 700–800 °C for several hours and then gradual cooling. It makes the steel softer and more workable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verhoeven_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Verhoeven-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Steel_pd.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="420" height="362" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Steel_pd.svg/400px-Steel_pd.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Steel_pd.svg/600px-Steel_pd.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Steel_pd.svg/800px-Steel_pd.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Steel_pd.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iron-carbon phase diagram</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metallurgical">Metallurgical</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">Steel</a></div>
<p>Iron is the most widely used of all the metals, accounting for over 90% of worldwide metal production. Its low cost and high strength make it indispensable in engineering applications such as the construction of machinery and <a href="/wiki/Machine_tool" title="Machine tool">machine tools</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">automobiles</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hull_(ship)" title="Hull (ship)">hulls</a> of large <a href="/wiki/Ship" title="Ship">ships</a>, and structural components for buildings. Since pure iron is quite soft, it is most commonly combined with alloying elements to make steel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood10701_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood10701-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>α-Iron is a fairly soft metal that can dissolve only a small concentration of carbon (no more than 0.021% by mass at 910 °C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Austenite" title="Austenite">Austenite</a> (γ-iron) is similarly soft and metallic but can dissolve considerably more carbon (as much as 2.04% by mass at 1146 °C). This form of iron is used in the type of <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steel</a> used for making cutlery, and hospital and food-service equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metallo_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Metallo-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Commercially available iron is classified based on purity and the abundance of additives. <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">Pig iron</a> has 3.5–4.5% carbon<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msts_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-msts-127">[127]</a></sup> and contains varying amounts of contaminants such as <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, silicon and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>. Pig iron is not a saleable product, but rather an intermediate step in the production of cast iron and steel. The reduction of contaminants in pig iron that negatively affect material properties, such as sulfur and phosphorus, yields cast iron containing 2–4% carbon, 1–6% silicon, and small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1073_122-9"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1073-122">[122]</a></sup> Pig iron has a <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a> in the range of 1420–1470 K, which is lower than either of its two main components, and makes it the first product to be melted when carbon and iron are heated together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1075_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1075-15">[15]</a></sup> Its mechanical properties vary greatly and depend on the form the carbon takes in the alloy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1074_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1074-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>"White" cast irons contain their carbon in the form of <a href="/wiki/Cementite" title="Cementite">cementite</a>, or iron carbide (Fe<sub>3</sub>C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1074_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1074-11">[11]</a></sup> This hard, brittle compound dominates the mechanical properties of white cast irons, rendering them hard, but unresistant to shock. The broken surface of a white cast iron is full of fine facets of the broken iron-carbide, a very pale, silvery, shiny material, hence the appellation. Cooling a mixture of iron with 0.8% carbon slowly below 723 °C to room temperature results in separate, alternating layers of cementite and α-iron, which is soft and malleable and is called <a href="/wiki/Pearlite" title="Pearlite">pearlite</a> for its appearance. Rapid cooling, on the other hand, does not allow time for this separation and creates hard and brittle <a href="/wiki/Martensite" title="Martensite">martensite</a>. The steel can then be tempered by reheating to a temperature in between, changing the proportions of pearlite and martensite. The end product below 0.8% carbon content is a pearlite-αFe mixture, and that above 0.8% carbon content is a pearlite-cementite mixture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1074_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1074-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Gray_iron" title="Gray iron">gray iron</a> the carbon exists as separate, fine flakes of <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a>, and also renders the material brittle due to the sharp edged flakes of graphite that produce <a href="/wiki/Stress_concentration" title="Stress concentration">stress concentration</a> sites within the material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hashemi_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hashemi-128">[128]</a></sup> A newer variant of gray iron, referred to as <a href="/wiki/Ductile_iron" title="Ductile iron">ductile iron</a> is specially treated with trace amounts of <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> to alter the shape of graphite to spheroids, or nodules, reducing the stress concentrations and vastly increasing the toughness and strength of the material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hashemi_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hashemi-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">Wrought iron</a> contains less than 0.25% carbon but large amounts of slag that give it a fibrous characteristic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msts_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-msts-127">[127]</a></sup> It is a tough, malleable product, but not as fusible as pig iron. If honed to an edge, it loses it quickly. Wrought iron is characterized by the presence of fine fibers of <a href="/wiki/Slag" title="Slag">slag</a> entrapped within the metal. Wrought iron is more corrosion resistant than steel. It has been almost completely replaced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mild_steel" title="Mild steel">mild steel</a> for traditional "wrought iron" products and <a href="/wiki/Blacksmith" title="Blacksmith">blacksmithing</a>.
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; clear:left; margin-left:1em; margin-top:0;">
