<p>Mild steel corrodes more readily than wrought iron, but is cheaper and more widely available. <a href="/wiki/Carbon_steel" title="Carbon steel">Carbon steel</a> contains 2.0% carbon or less,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kts_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-kts-130">[130]</a></sup> with small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, and silicon. <a href="/wiki/Alloy_steel" title="Alloy steel">Alloy steels</a> contain varying amounts of carbon as well as other metals, such as <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a>, nickel, <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a>, etc. Their alloy content raises their cost, and so they are usually only employed for specialist uses. One common alloy steel, though, is <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steel</a>. Recent developments in ferrous metallurgy have produced a growing range of microalloyed steels, also termed '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HSLA_steel" title="HSLA steel">HSLA</a>' or high-strength, low alloy steels, containing tiny additions to produce high strengths and often spectacular toughness at minimal cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kts_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-kts-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-md_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-md-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ironattenuation.PNG"><img alt="A graph of attenuation coefficient vs. energy between 1 meV and 100 keV for several photon scattering mechanisms." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="950" data-file-width="1400" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Ironattenuation.PNG/220px-Ironattenuation.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Ironattenuation.PNG/330px-Ironattenuation.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Ironattenuation.PNG/440px-Ironattenuation.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ironattenuation.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">Photon</a> <a href="/wiki/Mass_attenuation_coefficient" title="Mass attenuation coefficient">mass attenuation coefficient</a> for iron.</div></div></div>
<p>Apart from traditional applications, iron is also used for protection from ionizing radiation. Although it is lighter than another traditional protection material, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, it is much stronger mechanically. The attenuation of radiation as a function of energy is shown in the graph.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>The main disadvantage of iron and steel is that pure iron, and most of its alloys, suffer badly from <a href="/wiki/Rust" title="Rust">rust</a> if not protected in some way, a cost amounting to over 1% of the world's economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1076_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1076-134">[134]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paint" title="Paint">Painting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galvanization" title="Galvanization">galvanization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivation</a>, plastic coating and <a href="/wiki/Bluing_(steel)" title="Bluing (steel)">bluing</a> are all used to protect iron from rust by excluding <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> and oxygen or by <a href="/wiki/Cathodic_protection" title="Cathodic protection">cathodic protection</a>. The mechanism of the rusting of iron is as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1076_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1076-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>Cathode: 3 O<sub>2</sub> + 6 H<sub>2</sub>O + 12 e<sup>−</sup> → 12 OH<sup>−</sup></dd>
<dd>Anode: 4 Fe → 4 Fe<sup>2+</sup> + 8 e<sup>−</sup>; 4 Fe<sup>2+</sup> → 4 Fe<sup>3+</sup> + 4 e<sup>−</sup></dd>
<dd>Overall: 4 Fe + 3 O<sub>2</sub> + 6 H<sub>2</sub>O → 4 Fe<sup>3+</sup> + 12 OH<sup>−</sup> → 4 Fe(OH)<sub>3</sub> or 4 FeO(OH) + 4 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>The electrolyte is usually <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_sulfate" title="Iron(II) sulfate">iron(II) sulfate</a> in urban areas (formed when atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> attacks iron), and salt particles in the atmosphere in seaside areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1076_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1076-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iron_compounds">Iron compounds</span></h3>
<p>Although the dominant use of iron is in metallurgy, iron compounds are also pervasive in industry. Iron catalysts are traditionally used in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haber-Bosch_process" title="Haber-Bosch process">Haber-Bosch process</a> for the production of ammonia and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fischer-Tropsch_process" title="Fischer-Tropsch process">Fischer-Tropsch process</a> for conversion of carbon monoxide to <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> for fuels and lubricants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Powdered iron in an acidic solvent was used in the <a href="/wiki/Bechamp_reduction" title="Bechamp reduction">Bechamp reduction</a> the reduction of <a href="/wiki/Nitrobenzene" title="Nitrobenzene">nitrobenzene</a> to <a href="/wiki/Aniline" title="Aniline">aniline</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_chloride" title="Iron(III) chloride">Iron(III) chloride</a> finds use in water purification and <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">sewage treatment</a>, in the dyeing of cloth, as a coloring agent in paints, as an additive in animal feed, and as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_etching" title="Industrial etching">etchant</a> for <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Printed_circuit_board" title="Printed circuit board">printed circuit boards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-137">[137]</a></sup> It can also be dissolved in alcohol to form tincture of iron, which is used as a medicine to stop bleeding in <a href="/wiki/Domestic_canary" title="Domestic canary">canaries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_sulfate" title="Iron(II) sulfate">Iron(II) sulfate</a> is used as a precursor to other iron compounds. It is also used to <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduce</a> chromate in cement. It is used to fortify foods and treat <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_deficiency_anemia" title="Iron deficiency anemia">iron deficiency anemia</a>. <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_sulfate" title="Iron(III) sulfate">Iron(III) sulfate</a> is used in settling minute sewage particles in tank water. <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_chloride" title="Iron(II) chloride">Iron(II) chloride</a> is used as a reducing flocculating agent, in the formation of iron complexes and magnetic iron oxides, and as a reducing agent in organic synthesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_and_pathological_role">Biological and pathological role</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_iron_metabolism" title="Human iron metabolism">Human iron metabolism</a></div>
<p>Iron is required for life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Iron%E2%80%93sulfur_cluster" title="Iron–sulfur cluster">iron–sulfur clusters</a> are pervasive and include <a href="/wiki/Nitrogenase" title="Nitrogenase">nitrogenase</a>, the enzymes responsible for biological <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">nitrogen fixation</a>. Iron-containing proteins participate in transport, storage and used of oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup> Iron proteins are involved in <a href="/wiki/Electron_transfer" title="Electron transfer">electron transfer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1098_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1098-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heme_b.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="685" data-file-width="620" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Heme_b.svg/220px-Heme_b.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Heme_b.svg/330px-Heme_b.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Heme_b.svg/440px-Heme_b.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heme_b.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of <a href="/wiki/Heme" title="Heme">Heme b</a>; in the protein additional <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a>(s) would be attached to Fe.</div></div></div>
<p>Examples of iron-containing proteins in higher organisms include hemoglobin, <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome" title="Cytochrome">cytochrome</a> (see <a href="/wiki/High-valent_iron" title="High-valent iron">high-valent iron</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Catalase" title="Catalase">catalase</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> The average adult human contains about 0.005% body weight of iron, or about four grams, of which three quarters is in hemoglobin – a level that remains constant despite only about one milligram of iron being absorbed each day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1098_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1098-141">[141]</a></sup> because the human body recycles its hemoglobin for the iron content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biochemistry">Biochemistry</span></h3>
<p>Iron acquisition poses a problem for aerobic organisms because ferric iron is poorly soluble near neutral pH. Thus, these organisms have developed means to absorb iron as complexes, sometimes taking up ferrous iron before oxidising it back to ferric iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup> In particular, bacteria have evolved very high-affinity <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sequester" title="wikt:sequester">sequestering</a> agents called <a href="/wiki/Siderophore" title="Siderophore">siderophores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>After uptake in human <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a>, iron storage is precisely regulated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> A major component of this regulation is the protein <a href="/wiki/Transferrin" title="Transferrin">transferrin</a>, which binds iron ions absorbed from the <a href="/wiki/Duodenum" title="Duodenum">duodenum</a> and carries it in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bloodstream" title="Bloodstream">blood</a> to cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Transferrin contains Fe<sup>3+</sup> in the middle of a distorted octahedron, bonded to one nitrogen, three oxygens and a chelating <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> anion that traps the Fe<sup>3+</sup> ion: it has such a high <a href="/wiki/Stability_constants_of_complexes" title="Stability constants of complexes">stability constant</a> that it is very effective at taking up Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions even from the most stable complexes. At the bone marrow, transferrin is reduced from Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> and stored as <a href="/wiki/Ferritin" title="Ferritin">ferritin</a> to be incorporated into hemoglobin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1098_141-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1098-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most commonly known and studied <a href="/wiki/Bioinorganic_chemistry" title="Bioinorganic chemistry">bioinorganic</a> iron compounds (biological iron molecules) are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heme_proteins" title="Heme proteins">heme proteins</a>: examples are <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_P450" title="Cytochrome P450">cytochrome P450</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup> These compounds participate in transporting gases, building <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enzymes" title="Enzymes">enzymes</a>, and transferring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrons" title="Electrons">electrons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1098_141-3"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1098-141">[141]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metalloproteins" title="Metalloproteins">Metalloproteins</a> are a group of proteins with metal ion <a href="/wiki/Cofactor_(biochemistry)" title="Cofactor (biochemistry)">cofactors</a>. Some examples of iron metalloproteins are <a href="/wiki/Ferritin" title="Ferritin">ferritin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rubredoxin" title="Rubredoxin">rubredoxin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1098_141-4"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1098-141">[141]</a></sup> Many enzymes vital to life contain iron, such as <a href="/wiki/Catalase" title="Catalase">catalase</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boon_b_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boon_b-149">[149]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipoxygenases" title="Lipoxygenases">lipoxygenases</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PUB00005162_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-PUB00005162-150">[150]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IRE-BP" title="IRE-BP">IRE-BP</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid8070415_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid8070415-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hemoglobin is an oxygen carrier that occurs in <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a> and contributes their color, transporting oxygen in the arteries from the lungs to the muscles where it is transferred to <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a>, which stores it until it is needed for the metabolic oxidation of <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>, generating energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-8"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup> Here the hemoglobin binds to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, produced when glucose is oxidized, which is transported through the veins by hemoglobin (predominantly as <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a> anions) back to the lungs where it is exhaled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1098_141-5"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1098-141">[141]</a></sup> In hemoglobin, the iron is in one of four <a href="/wiki/Heme" title="Heme">heme</a> groups and has six possible coordination sites; four are occupied by nitrogen atoms in a <a href="/wiki/Porphyrin" title="Porphyrin">porphyrin</a> ring, the fifth by an <a href="/wiki/Imidazole" title="Imidazole">imidazole</a> nitrogen in a <a href="/wiki/Histidine" title="Histidine">histidine</a> residue of one of the protein chains attached to the heme group, and the sixth is reserved for the oxygen molecule it can reversibly bind to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1098_141-6"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1098-141">[141]</a></sup> When hemoglobin is not attached to oxygen (and is then called deoxyhemoglobin), the Fe<sup>2+</sup> ion at the center of the <a href="/wiki/Heme" title="Heme">heme</a> group (in the hydrophobic protein interior) is in a high-spin configuration. It is thus too large to fit inside the porphyrin ring, which bends instead into a dome with the Fe<sup>2+</sup> ion about 55 picometers above it. In this configuration, the sixth coordination site reserved for the oxygen is blocked by another histidine residue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1098_141-7"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1098-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>When deoxyhemoglobin picks up an oxygen molecule, this histidine residue moves away and returns once the oxygen is securely attached to form a <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bond</a> with it. This results in the Fe<sup>2+</sup> ion switching to a low-spin configuration, resulting in a 20% decrease in ionic radius so that now it can fit into the porphyrin ring, which becomes planar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1098_141-8"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1098-141">[141]</a></sup> (Additionally, this hydrogen bonding results in the tilting of the oxygen molecule, resulting in a Fe–O–O bond angle of around 120° that avoids the formation of Fe–O–Fe or Fe–O<sub>2</sub>–Fe bridges that would lead to electron transfer, the oxidation of Fe<sup>2+</sup> to Fe<sup>3+</sup>, and the destruction of hemoglobin.) This results in a movement of all the protein chains that leads to the other subunits of hemoglobin changing shape to a form with larger oxygen affinity. Thus, when deoxyhemoglobin takes up oxygen, its affinity for more oxygen increases, and vice versa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1098_141-9"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1098-141">[141]</a></sup> Myoglobin, on the other hand, contains only one heme group and hence this cooperative effect cannot occur. Thus, while hemoglobin is almost saturated with oxygen in the high partial pressures of oxygen found in the lungs, its affinity for oxygen is much lower than that of myoglobin, which oxygenates even at low partial pressures of oxygen found in muscle tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1098_141-10"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1098-141">[141]</a></sup> As described by the <a href="/wiki/Bohr_effect" title="Bohr effect">Bohr effect</a> (named after <a href="/wiki/Christian_Bohr" title="Christian Bohr">Christian Bohr</a>, the father of <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a>), the oxygen affinity of hemoglobin diminishes in the presence of carbon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1098_141-11"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1098-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carboxyhemoglobin_from_1AJ9.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1011" data-file-width="1055" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Carboxyhemoglobin_from_1AJ9.png/220px-Carboxyhemoglobin_from_1AJ9.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Carboxyhemoglobin_from_1AJ9.png/330px-Carboxyhemoglobin_from_1AJ9.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Carboxyhemoglobin_from_1AJ9.png/440px-Carboxyhemoglobin_from_1AJ9.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carboxyhemoglobin_from_1AJ9.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A heme unit of human <a href="/wiki/Carboxyhemoglobin" title="Carboxyhemoglobin">carboxyhemoglobin</a>, showing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbonyl_ligand" title="Carbonyl ligand">carbonyl ligand</a> at the apical position, <i>trans</i> to the histidine residue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">Carbon monoxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_trifluoride" title="Phosphorus trifluoride">phosphorus trifluoride</a> are poisonous to humans because they bind to hemoglobin similarly to oxygen, but with much more strength, so that oxygen can no longer be transported throughout the body. Hemoglobin bound to carbon monoxide is known as <a href="/wiki/Carboxyhemoglobin" title="Carboxyhemoglobin">carboxyhemoglobin</a>. This effect also plays a minor role in the toxicity of <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanide</a>, but there the major effect is by far its interference with the proper functioning of the electron transport protein cytochrome a.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1098_141-12"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1098-141">[141]</a></sup> The cytochrome proteins also involve heme groups and are involved in the metabolic oxidation of glucose by oxygen. The sixth coordination site is then occupied by either another imidazole nitrogen or a <a href="/wiki/Methionine" title="Methionine">methionine</a> sulfur, so that these proteins are largely inert to oxygen – with the exception of cytochrome a, which bonds directly to oxygen and thus is very easily poisoned by cyanide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1098_141-13"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1098-141">[141]</a></sup> Here, the electron transfer takes place as the iron remains in low spin but changes between the +2 and +3 oxidation states. Since the reduction potential of each step is slightly greater than the previous one, the energy is released step-by-step and can thus be stored in <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a>. Cytochrome a is slightly distinct, as it occurs at the mitochondrial membrane, binds directly to oxygen, and transports protons as well as electrons, as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1098_141-14"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1098-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>4 Cytc<sup>2+</sup> + O<sub>2</sub> + 8H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">inside</sub></span> → 4 Cytc<sup>3+</sup> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O + 4H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">outside</sub></span></dd></dl>
<p>Although the heme proteins are the most important class of iron-containing proteins, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron-sulfur_protein" title="Iron-sulfur protein">iron-sulfur proteins</a> are also very important, being involved in electron transfer, which is possible since iron can exist stably in either the +2 or +3 oxidation states. These have one, two, four, or eight iron atoms that are each approximately tetrahedrally coordinated to four sulfur atoms; because of this tetrahedral coordination, they always have high-spin iron. The simplest of such compounds is <a href="/wiki/Rubredoxin" title="Rubredoxin">rubredoxin</a>, which has only one iron atom coordinated to four sulfur atoms from <a href="/wiki/Cysteine" title="Cysteine">cysteine</a> residues in the surrounding peptide chains. Another important class of iron-sulfur proteins is the <a href="/wiki/Ferredoxin" title="Ferredoxin">ferredoxins</a>, which have multiple iron atoms. Transferrin does not belong to either of these classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1098_141-15"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1098-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ability of sea <a href="/wiki/Mussel" title="Mussel">mussels</a> to maintain their grip on rocks in the ocean is facilitated by their use of <a href="/wiki/Organometallic_chemistry" title="Organometallic chemistry">organometallic</a> iron-based bonds in their protein-rich <a href="/wiki/Cuticle" title="Cuticle">cuticles</a>. Based on synthetic replicas, the presence of iron in these structures increased <a href="/wiki/Elastic_modulus" title="Elastic modulus">elastic modulus</a> 770 times, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tensile_strength" title="Tensile strength">tensile strength</a> 58 times, and <a href="/wiki/Toughness" title="Toughness">toughness</a> 92 times. The amount of stress required to permanently damage them increased 76 times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_and_diet">Health and diet</span></h3>
<p>Iron is pervasive, but particularly rich sources of dietary iron include <a href="/wiki/Red_meat" title="Red meat">red meat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oyster" title="Oyster">oysters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lentil" title="Lentil">lentils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">beans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leaf_vegetable" title="Leaf vegetable">leaf vegetables</a>, <a href="/wiki/Watercress" title="Watercress">watercress</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">tofu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chickpea" title="Chickpea">chickpeas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black-eyed_peas" title="Black-eyed peas">black-eyed peas</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blackstrap_molasses" title="Blackstrap molasses">blackstrap molasses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-9"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bread" title="Bread">Bread</a> and <a href="/wiki/Breakfast_cereal" title="Breakfast cereal">breakfast cereals</a> are sometimes specifically fortified with iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-10"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>Iron provided by <a href="/wiki/Dietary_supplement" title="Dietary supplement">dietary supplements</a> is often found as <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_fumarate" title="Iron(II) fumarate">iron(II) fumarate</a>, although <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_sulfate" title="Iron(II) sulfate">iron(II) sulfate</a> is cheaper and is absorbed equally well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_137-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-137">[137]</a></sup> Elemental iron, or reduced iron, despite being absorbed at only one-third to two-thirds the efficiency (relative to iron sulfate),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> is often added to foods such as breakfast cereals or enriched wheat flour. Iron is most available to the body when <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">chelated</a> to amino acids<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11377130_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11377130-156">[156]</a></sup> and is also available for use as a common <a href="/wiki/Iron_supplement" title="Iron supplement">iron supplement</a>. <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">Glycine</a>, the cheapest and most common amino acid is most often used to produce iron glycinate supplements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ashmead_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ashmead-157">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_recommendations">Dietary recommendations</span></h3>
<p>The U.S. Institute of Medicine (IOM) updated Estimated Average Requirements (EARs) and Recommended Dietary Allowances (RDAs) for iron in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-11"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup> The current EAR for iron for women ages 14–18 is 7.9 mg/day, 8.1 for ages 19–50 and 5.0 thereafter (post menopause). For men the EAR is 6.0 mg/day for ages 19 and up. The RDA is 15.0 mg/day for women ages 15–18, 18.0 for 19–50 and 8.0 thereafter. For men, 8.0 mg/day for ages 19 and up. RDAs are higher than EARs so as to identify amounts that will cover people with higher than average requirements. RDA for pregnancy is 27 mg/day and, for lactation, 9 mg/day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-12"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup> For children ages 1–3 years 7 mg/day, 10 for ages 4–8 and 8 for ages 9–13. As for safety, the IOM also sets <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tolerable_upper_intake_level" title="Tolerable upper intake level">Tolerable upper intake levels</a> (ULs) for vitamins and minerals when evidence is sufficient. In the case of iron the UL is set at 45 mg/day. Collectively the EARs, RDAs and ULs are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake" title="Dietary Reference Intake">Dietary Reference Intakes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Food_Safety_Authority" title="European Food Safety Authority">European Food Safety Authority</a> (EFSA) refers to the collective set of information as Dietary Reference Values, with Population Reference Intake (PRI) instead of RDA, and Average Requirement instead of EAR. AI and UL defined the same as in United States. For women the PRI is 13 mg/day ages 15–17 years, 16 mg/day for women ages 18 and up who are premenopausal and 11 mg/day postmenopausal. For pregnancy and lactation, 16 mg/day. For men the PRI is 11 mg/day ages 15 and older. For children ages 1 to 14 the PRI increases from 7 to 11 mg/day. The PRIs are higher than the U.S. RDAs, with the exception of pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> The EFSA reviewed the same safety question did not establish a UL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>Infants may require iron supplements if they are bottle-fed cow's milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> Frequent <a href="/wiki/Blood_donation" title="Blood donation">blood donors</a> are at risk of low iron levels and are often advised to supplement their iron intake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>For U.S. food and dietary supplement labeling purposes the amount in a serving is expressed as a percent of Daily Value (%DV). For iron labeling purposes 100% of the Daily Value was 18 mg, and as of May 27, 2016 remained unchanged at 18 mg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FedReg_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FedReg-163">[163]</a></sup> A table of all of the old and new adult Daily Values is provided at <a href="/wiki/Reference_Daily_Intake" title="Reference Daily Intake">Reference Daily Intake</a>. The original deadline to be in compliance was July 28, 2018, but on September 29, 2017 the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> released a proposed rule that extended the deadline to January 1, 2020 for large companies and January 1, 2021 for small companies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FDAdelay_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FDAdelay-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deficiency">Deficiency</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Iron_deficiency" title="Iron deficiency">Iron deficiency</a></div>
<p>Iron deficiency is the most common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nutritional_deficiency" title="Nutritional deficiency">nutritional deficiency</a> in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-13"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> When loss of iron is not adequately compensated by adequate dietary iron intake, a state of <a href="/wiki/Latent_iron_deficiency" title="Latent iron deficiency">latent iron deficiency</a> occurs, which over time leads to <a href="/wiki/Iron-deficiency_anemia" title="Iron-deficiency anemia">iron-deficiency anemia</a> if left untreated, which is characterised by an insufficient number of red blood cells and an insufficient amount of hemoglobin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> Children, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-menopausal" title="Pre-menopausal">pre-menopausal</a> women (women of child-bearing age), and people with poor diet are most susceptible to the disease. Most cases of iron-deficiency anemia are mild, but if not treated can cause problems like fast or irregular heartbeat, complications during pregnancy, and delayed growth in infants and children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Excess">Excess</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Iron_overload" title="Iron overload">Iron overload</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Human_iron_metabolism" title="Human iron metabolism">Iron uptake</a> is tightly regulated by the human body, which has no regulated physiological means of excreting iron. Only small amounts of iron are lost daily due to mucosal and skin epithelial cell sloughing, so control of iron levels is primarily accomplished by regulating uptake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> Regulation of iron uptake is impaired in some people as a result of a <a href="/wiki/Genetic_disorder" title="Genetic disorder">genetic defect</a> that maps to the HLA-H gene region on <a href="/wiki/Chromosome_6" title="Chromosome 6">chromosome 6</a> and leads to abnormally low levels of <a href="/wiki/Hepcidin" title="Hepcidin">hepcidin</a>, a key regulator of the entry of iron into the circulatory system in mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12663437_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12663437-171">[171]</a></sup> In these people, excessive iron intake can result in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_overload_disorder" title="Iron overload disorder">iron overload disorders</a>, known medically as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemochromatosis" title="Hemochromatosis">hemochromatosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-14"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup> Many people have an undiagnosed genetic susceptibility to iron overload, and are not aware of a family history of the problem. For this reason, people should not take iron supplements unless they suffer from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_deficiency_(medicine)" title="Iron deficiency (medicine)">iron deficiency</a> and have consulted a doctor. Hemochromatosis is estimated to be the cause of 0.3 to 0.8% of all metabolic diseases of Caucasians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>Overdoses of ingested iron can cause excessive levels of free iron in the blood. High blood levels of free ferrous iron react with <a href="/wiki/Peroxide" title="Peroxide">peroxides</a> to produce highly reactive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radical" title="Free radical">free radicals</a> that can damage <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proteins" title="Proteins">proteins</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipids" title="Lipids">lipids</a>, and other cellular components. Iron toxicity occurs when the cell contains free iron, which generally occurs when iron levels exceed the availability of <a href="/wiki/Transferrin" title="Transferrin">transferrin</a> to bind the iron. Damage to the cells of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_gastrointestinal_tract" title="Human gastrointestinal tract">gastrointestinal tract</a> can also prevent them from regulating iron absorption, leading to further increases in blood levels. Iron typically damages cells in the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> and elsewhere, causing adverse effects that include <a href="/wiki/Coma" title="Coma">coma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_acidosis" title="Metabolic acidosis">metabolic acidosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shock_(circulatory)" title="Shock (circulatory)">shock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liver_failure" title="Liver failure">liver failure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coagulopathy" title="Coagulopathy">coagulopathy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adult_respiratory_distress_syndrome" title="Adult respiratory distress syndrome">adult respiratory distress syndrome</a>, long-term organ damage, and even death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cheney_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cheney-173">[173]</a></sup> Humans experience iron toxicity when the iron exceeds 20 milligrams for every kilogram of body mass; 60 milligrams per kilogram is considered a <a href="/wiki/Lethal_dose" title="Lethal dose">lethal dose</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emed-topic285_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-emed-topic285-174">[174]</a></sup> Overconsumption of iron, often the result of children eating large quantities of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrous_sulfate" title="Ferrous sulfate">ferrous sulfate</a> tablets intended for adult consumption, is one of the most common toxicological causes of death in children under six.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emed-topic285_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-emed-topic285-174">[174]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake" title="Dietary Reference Intake">Dietary Reference Intake</a> (DRI) sets the Tolerable Upper Intake Level (UL) for adults at 45 mg/day. For children under fourteen years old the UL is 40 mg/day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IOM_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-IOM-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>The medical management of iron toxicity is complicated, and can include use of a specific <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">chelating</a> agent called <a href="/wiki/Deferoxamine" title="Deferoxamine">deferoxamine</a> to bind and expel excess iron from the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cheney_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cheney-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21102602_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21102602-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer">Cancer</span></h3>
<p>The role of iron in cancer defense can be described as a "double-edged sword" because of its pervasive presence in non-pathological processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> People having <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">chemotherapy</a> may develop iron deficiency and <a href="/wiki/Anemia" title="Anemia">anemia</a>, for which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intravenous" title="Intravenous">intravenous</a> iron therapy is used to restore iron levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beguin_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-beguin-179">[179]</a></sup> Iron overload, which may occur from high consumption of red meat,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-15"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup> may initiate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumor" title="Tumor">tumor</a> growth and increase susceptibility to cancer onset,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beguin_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-beguin-179">[179]</a></sup> particularly for <a href="/wiki/Colorectal_cancer" title="Colorectal cancer">colorectal cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpi_5-16"><a href="#cite_note-lpi-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-beguin-179"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-beguin_179-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-beguin_179-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Beguin, Y; Aapro, M; Ludwig, H; Mizzen, L; Osterborg, A (2014). <a class="external text" href="http://www.croh-online.com/article/S1040-8428(13)00230-8/fulltext" rel="nofollow">"Epidemiological and nonclinical studies investigating effects of iron in carcinogenesis--a critical review"</a>. <i>Critical Reviews in Oncology/Hematology</i>. <b>89</b> (1): 1–15. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.critrevonc.2013.10.008" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.critrevonc.2013.10.008</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" title="PubMed Identifier">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/24275533" rel="nofollow">24275533</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Critical+Reviews+in+Oncology%2FHematology&amp;rft.atitle=Epidemiological+and+nonclinical+studies+investigating+effects+of+iron+in+carcinogenesis--a+critical+review&amp;rft.volume=89&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=1-15&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.critrevonc.2013.10.008&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F24275533&amp;rft.aulast=Beguin&amp;rft.aufirst=Y&amp;rft.au=Aapro%2C+M&amp;rft.au=Ludwig%2C+H&amp;rft.au=Mizzen%2C+L&amp;rft.au=Osterborg%2C+A&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.croh-online.com%2Farticle%2FS1040-8428%2813%2900230-8%2Ffulltext&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIron"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
